<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Centrioles- Centrioles are paired barrel-shaped organelles located in the cytoplasm of animal cells near the nuclear envelope. Centrioles play a role in organizing microtubules that serve as the cell's skeletal system. They help determine the locations of the nucleus and other organelles within the cell. They also form spindle fibres.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cellular-communication">CELLULAR COMMUNICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cellular-communication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are different systems for communicating with the internal and external environments. Internal communication involves mainly the nervous and endocrine systems; these are important in the maintenance of homeostasis and regulation of vital body functions. Communication with the external environment involves the special senses, and verbal and non-verbal activities, and all of these also depend on the nervous system.</p>
<p>All communication systems involve receiving, collating and responding to appropriate information.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="internal-communication">Internal communication<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#internal-communication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="communication-and-the-nervous-system">Communication and the nervous system<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#communication-and-the-nervous-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The nervous system is a rapid communication system. The main components are shown in Figure 1.8.</p>
<p>The central nervous system consists of:</p>
<ul>
<li>The brain, situated inside the skull</li>
<li>The spinal cord, which extends from the base of the skull to the lumbar region and is protected from injury by the bones of the spinal column.</li>
</ul>
<p>The peripheral nervous system is a network of nerve fibres, which are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Sensory or afferent, providing the brain with 'input' from organs and tissues, or</li>
<li>Motor or efferent, which convey nerve impulses carrying 'output' from the brain to effector organs: the muscles and glands.</li>
</ul>
<p>The somatic (common) senses are pain, touch, heat and cold, and they arise following stimulation of specialized sensory receptors at nerve endings found throughout the skin.</p>
<p>Communication and the endocrine system- The endocrine system consists of a number of endocrine glands situated in different parts of the body. They synthesise and secrete chemical messengers called hormones that circulate round the body in the blood. Hormones stimulate target glands or tissues, influencing metabolic and other cellular activities and regulating body growth and maturation. Endocrine glands detect and respond to levels of particular substances in the blood, including specific hormones. Changes in blood hormone levels are controlled by negative feedback mechanisms (Fig. 1.3).</p>
<p>The endocrine system provides slower and more precise control of body functions than the nervous system.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="communication-with-the-external-environment">Communication with the external environment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#communication-with-the-external-environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Special senses- These senses arise following stimulation of specialized sensory receptor cells located in sensory organs or tissues in the head.</p>
<p>Verbal communication- Sound is a means of communication and is produced in the larynx as a result of blowing air through the space between the vocal cords during expiration. Speech is the manipulation of sound by contraction of the muscles of the throat and cheeks, and movements of the tongue and lower jaw.</p>
<p>Non-verbal communication- Posture and movements are associated with non-verbal communication, e.g. nodding the head and shrugging the shoulders.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="intracellular-communication-signaling">Intracellular communication (signaling)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#intracellular-communication-signaling" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cellular receptors can broadly categorize into internal receptors, cell-surface receptors, ion channel receptors, G-protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs), and enzyme-linked receptors. While most cell receptor binding is by a chemical ligand, two notable exceptions are viruses which are pathogenic that can bind host cellular receptors to infect a cell, and bacterial components, which can bind receptors on immune cells to cause an immune response. Communication between cells is called intercellular signaling, and communication within a cell is called intracellular signaling.</p>
<p>Chemical signals are released by a signaling cell and received by a target cell. Target cells have proteins called receptors, which bind to signaling molecules and cause a response. Signaling molecules that bind to receptors are called ligands. Ligands and receptors are specific for each other; a receptor will typically bind only to its specific ligand. There are four categories of chemical signaling found in multicellular organisms: autocrine signaling, paracrine signaling, endocrine signaling, and direct signaling across gap junctions</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-cell-receptors">Types of cell receptors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-cell-receptors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(i). Receptors that are ion channels- Facilitated diffusion of ions takes place through proteins, or assemblies of proteins, embedded in the plasma membrane. These transmembrane proteins form a water-filled channel through</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>which the ion can pass down its concentration gradient. The transmembrane channels that permit facilitated diffusion can be opened or closed. They are said to be "gated"; some types of gated ion channels:</p>
<ul>
<li>ligand-gated</li>
<li>mechanically-gated</li>
<li>voltage-gated</li>
<li>light-gated</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ligand-gated-ion-channels">Ligand-gated ion channels<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ligand-gated-ion-channels" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Many ion channels open or close in response to binding a small signaling molecule or "ligand". Some ion channels are gated by extracellular ligands; some by intracellular ligands. In both cases, the ligand is not the substance that is transported when the channel opens.</p>
<p><strong>External ligands</strong> - External ligands (shown here in green) bind to a site on the extracellular side of the channel.</p>
<p><strong>Receptor-operated channels</strong> - These are found in the post-synaptic membrane and open when they bind the neurotransmitter. Example: NMDA receptors. G-protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs). These are not channels but they trigger a release of Ca from the endoplasmic reticulum as described above. They are activated by various hormones and neurotransmitters (as well as bitter substances on taste cells in the tongue).</p>
<p><strong>Examples: Acetylcholine (ACh)</strong>. The binding of the neurotransmitter acetylcholine at certain synapses opens channels that admit Na and initiate a nerve impulse or muscle contraction.</p>
<p><strong>Gamma amino butyric acid (GABA)</strong>. Binding of GABA at certain synapses designated GABA in the central nervous system admits Cl ions into the cell and inhibits the creation of a nerve impulse</p>
<p><strong>Internal ligands</strong> - Internal ligands bind to a site on the channel protein exposed to the cytosol. <strong>Examples</strong>: "Second messengers", like cyclic AMP (cAMP) and cyclic GMP (cGMP), regulate channels involved in the initiation of impulses in neurons responding to odors and light respectively. ATP is needed to open the channel that allows chloride (Cl⁻) and bicarbonate (HCO₃⁻) ions out of the cell. This channel is defective in patients with cystic fibrosis. Although the energy liberated by the hydrolysis of ATP is needed to open the channel, this is not an example of active transport; the ions diffuse through the open channel following their concentration gradient.</p>
<p><strong>(ii). G-protein-linked receptors</strong> bind to a ligand and activate an associated G-protein. The activated G-protein then interacts with a nearby membrane protein, which may be an ion channel or an enzyme. All G-protein-linked receptors have seven transmembrane domains, but each receptor has a specific extracellular domain and G-protein-binding site. G proteins are so-called because they bind the guanine nucleotides GDP and GTP. They are heterotrimers (i.e., made of three different subunits) associated with the inner surface of the plasma membrane and transmembrane receptors of hormones, etc. These are called G protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs). The three subunits are: Gα, which carries the binding site for the nucleotide. At least 20 different kinds of Gα molecules are found in mammalian cells, Gβ, and Gγ</p>
<p>In the inactive state, Gα has GDP in its binding site. When a hormone or other ligand binds to the associated GPCR, an allosteric change takes place in the receptor. This triggers an allosteric change in Gα causing GDP to leave and be replaced by GTP. GTP activates Gα causing it to dissociate from GβGγ (which remain linked as a dimer). Activated Gα in turn activates an effector molecule e.g. adenylyl cyclase - an enzyme in the inner face of the plasma membrane which catalyzes the conversion of ATP into the "second messenger" cyclic AMP (cAMP). Activated Gα is a GTPase so it quickly converts its GTP to GDP. This conversion, coupled with the return of the Gβ and Gγ subunits, restores the G protein to its inactive state.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="51a03ac1">Some Types of Gα Subunits<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#51a03ac1" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Gαs</strong> - This type stimulates (s = "stimulatory") adenylyl cyclase. It is associated with the receptors for many hormones</p>
<ul>
<li>adrenaline</li>
<li>glucagon</li>
<li>luteinizing hormone (LH)</li>
<li>parathyroid hormone (PTH)</li>
<li>adrenocorticotropic hormone (ACTH)</li>
</ul>
<p>Gαs is the target of the toxin liberated by Vibrio cholerae, the bacterium that causes cholera. Binding of cholera toxin to Gαs keeps it turned "on". The resulting continuous high levels of AMP causes a massive loss of salts from the cells of the intestinal epithelium. Massive amounts of water follow by osmosis causing a diarrhea that can be fatal if the salts and water are not quickly replaced.</p>
<p>Gαq - This activates phospholipase C₁ (PLC) which generates gas and other sugars:</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>inositol trisphosphate (IP)</li>
<li>diacylglycerol (DAG)</li>
</ul>
<p>Gα is found in G proteins coupled to receptors for vasopressin, thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH), and angiotensin.</p>
<p>Gαi- This inhibits (i = "inhibitory") adenylyl cyclase lowering the level of cAMP in the cell. Ga is activated by the receptor for somatostatin.</p>
<p>Gαt- The "t" is for transducin, the molecule responsible for generating a signal in the rods of the retina in response to light. Gα triggers the breakdown of cyclic GMP (cGMP).</p>
<p>Second messengers are molecules that relay signals received at receptors on the cell surface — such as the arrival of protein hormones, growth factors, etc. — to target molecules in the cytosol and/or nucleus. But in addition to their job as relay molecules, second messengers serve to greatly amplify the strength of the signal. Binding of a ligand to a single receptor at the cell surface may end up causing massive changes in the biochemical activities within the cell.</p>
<p>There are 3 major classes of second messengers:</p>
<ol>
<li>Cyclic nucleotides (e.g., cAMP and cGMP)</li>
<li>Inositol trisphosphate (IP) and diacylglycerol (DAG)</li>
<li>Calcium ions (Ca)</li>
</ol>
<p>Cyclic AMP (cAMP)- Some of the hormones that achieve their effects through cAMP as a second messenger:</p>
<ul>
<li>adrenaline</li>
<li>glucagon</li>
<li>luteinizing hormone (LH)</li>
</ul>
<p>Cyclic AMP is synthesized from ATP by the action of the enzyme adenylyl cyclase. Binding of the hormone to its receptor activates a G protein which, in turn, activates adenylyl cyclase.</p>
<p>The resulting rise in cAMP turns on the appropriate response in the cell by either (or both): changing the molecular activities in the cytosol, often using Protein Kinase A (PKA), a cAMP-dependent protein kinase that phosphorylates target proteins turning on a new pattern of gene transcription</p>
<p>Cyclic GMP (cGMP)- Cyclic GMP is synthesized from the nucleotide GTP using the enzyme guanylyl cyclase. Cyclic GMP serves as the second messenger for</p>
<ul>
<li>atrial natriuretic peptide (ANP)</li>
<li>nitric oxide (NO)</li>
<li>the response of the rods of the retina to light</li>
</ul>
<p>Some of the effects of cGMP are mediated through Protein Kinase G (PKG) — a cGMP- dependent protein kinase that phosphorylates target proteins in the cell. It seems to work through enzyme linked receptors.</p>
<p>Inositol trisphosphate (IP) and diacylglycerol (DAG)- Peptide and protein hormones like vasopressin, thyroid-stimulating hormone (TSH), and angiotensin and neurotransmitters like GABA bind to G protein-coupled receptors (GPCRs) that activate the intracellular enzyme phospholipase C (PLC).</p>
<p>As its name suggests, it hydrolyzes phospholipids — specifically phosphatidylinositol-4,5-bisphosphate (PIP) which is found in the inner layer of the plasma membrane. Hydrolysis of PIP yields two products: diacylglycerol (DAG): DAG remains in the inner layer of the plasma membrane. It recruits Protein Kinase C (PKC) — a calcium-dependent kinase that phosphorylates many other proteins that bring about the changes in the cell. As its name suggests, activation of PKC requires calcium ions. These are made available by the action of the other second messenger — IP3- inositol-1,4,5-trisphosphate (IP3): This soluble molecule diffuses through the cytosol and binds to receptors on the endoplasmic reticulum causing the release of calcium ions (Ca) into the cytosol. The rise in intracellular calcium triggers the response.</p>
<p>Example: The calcium rise is needed for NF-AT (the "nuclear factor of activated T cells") to turn on the appropriate genes in the nucleus. The remarkable ability of tacrolimus and cyclosporine to prevent graft rejection is due to their blocking this pathway. The binding of an antigen to its receptor on a B cell (the BCR) also generates the second messengers DAG and IP.</p>
<p>Calcium ions (Ca)- As the functions of IP and DAG indicate, calcium ions are also important intracellular messengers. In fact, calcium ions are probably the most widely used intracellular messengers.</p>
<p>In response to many different signals, a rise in the concentration of Ca in the cytosol triggers many types of events such as muscle contraction exocytosis, e.g. release of neurotransmitters at synapses (and essential for the long-term synaptic changes that produce Long-Term Potentiation (LTP) and Long-Term Depression (LTD); secretion of hormones like insulin activation of T cells and B cells when they bind antigen with their antigen receptors (TCRs and</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>BCRs respectively) adhesion of cells to the extracellular matrix (ECM) apoptosis a variety of biochemical changes mediated by Protein Kinase C (PKC).</p>
<p>Normally, the level of calcium in the cell is very low (~100 nM). There are two main depots of Ca²⁺ for the cell: The extracellular fluid (ECF made from blood), where the concentration is ~2 mM or 20,000 times higher than in the cytosol; the endoplasmic reticulum ("sarcoplasmic" reticulum in skeletal muscle).</p>
<p>However, its level in the cell can rise dramatically when channels in the plasma membrane open to allow it in from the extracellular fluid or from depots within the cell such as the endoplasmic reticulum and mitochondria.</p>
<p>Getting Ca²⁺ into (and out of) the cytosol- Voltage-gated channels open in response to a change in membrane potential, e.g. the depolarization of an action potential are found in excitable cells: skeletal muscle smooth muscle (These are the channels blocked by drugs, such as felodipine [Plendil®], used to treat high blood pressure. The influx of Ca contracts the smooth muscle walls of the arterioles, raising blood pressure. The drugs block this.) neurons. When the action potential reaches the presynaptic terminal, the influx of Ca triggers the release of the neurotransmitter. the taste cells that respond to salt. allow some 10 ions to flow in each second following the steep concentration gradient.</p>
<p>(iii). Enzyme-linked receptors are cell-surface receptors with intracellular domains that are associated with an enzyme. In some cases, the intracellular domain of the receptor itself is an enzyme. Other enzyme-linked receptors have a small intracellular domain that interacts directly with an enzyme. Enzyme-linked receptors normally have large extracellular and intracellular domains, but the membrane-spanning region consists of a single alpha-helix in the peptide strand. One example of an enzyme-linked receptor is the tyrosine kinase receptor. A kinase is an enzyme that transfers phosphate groups from ATP to another protein. The tyrosine kinase receptor transfers phosphate groups to tyrosine molecules. Tyrosine kinase receptors contain kinase - an enzyme which transfers phosphate molecules. In the case of tyrosine kinase, phosphate molecules are transferred to the amino acid tyrosine. When a ligand binds to the extracellular domain of a tyrosine kinase receptor, the receptor dimerises with its neighbouring receptor. Dimerisation triggers phosphorylation of the tyrosine molecules on the intracellular domain of the receptor. Phosphorylated tyrosine molecules are involved in many different signalling cascades which are crucial for various processes such as cell division and wound healing.</p>
<p>(iv). Internal receptors- intracellular (or internal) receptors are found inside the cell, in the cytoplasm or nucleus. Their ligands tend to be small, hydrophobic (i.e. lipid-soluble) molecules, which can diffuse across the plasma membrane by themselves. Once inside the cell, these ligands bind to their specific intracellular receptor to form a ligand-receptor complex, which will then bind to DNA in the nucleus and directly affect gene transcription and protein synthesis. Like cell surface receptors, intracellular receptors also have three core domains. These comprise a ligand-binding domain, DNA binding domain and an amino terminus (which interacts with the gene transcription machinery). Examples of ligands which bind to intracellular receptors include sex hormones, thyroid hormones and the fat-soluble vitamins. endogenous ligands bind to receptors to gain access into cells, but viruses can also do the same. Instead of receptor binding triggering a physiological signalling cascade, a virus binding to, and hijacking, a receptor facilitates infection by granting the virus entry into the cell. For example, HIV infects CD4 T cells and macrophages by binding to their CD4 receptor. SARS-CoV-2, the cause of COVID-19, enters cells by binding to angiotensin-converting enzyme 2, a receptor found in various organs including the lung, heart and kidneys.</p>
<p>Responses to the Signaling Pathway this include Gene Expression, Increase in Cellular Metabolism, Cell Growth, Cell Death</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="structure-of-deoxyribonucleic-acid-dna">STRUCTURE OF DEOXYRIBONUCLEIC ACID (DNA)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#structure-of-deoxyribonucleic-acid-dna" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a double strand of nucleotides arranged in a spiral (helix) which resembles a twisted ladder (Fig. 2.10). Chromosomes are clusters of DNA molecules consisting of functional subunits called genes. The nucleotides contain the sugar deoxyribose, phosphate groups and one of four bases: adenine [A], thymine [T], guanine [G] and cytosine [C]. A in one chain is paired with T in the other, and G with C. In this way, nucleotides are arranged in a precisely ordered manner in which one chain is complementary to the other. DNA acts as the template for protein synthesis and is stored safely in the nucleus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="ribonucleic-acid-rna">Ribonucleic acid (RNA)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ribonucleic-acid-rna" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a single-stranded chain of nucleotides which contains the sugar ribose instead of the deoxyribose found in DNA. It contains no thymine, but uses uracil [U] instead. It is synthesized in the nucleus from the DNA template, and</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>11</p>
<p>Studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>carries the message instructing synthesis of a new protein from the DNA (which cannot leave the nucleus) to the protein-synthesising apparatus in the cell cytoplasm.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-18d2a4a8.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-e8169c58.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="process-of-dna-replication-in-prokaryotes">PROCESS OF DNA REPLICATION IN PROKARYOTES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#process-of-dna-replication-in-prokaryotes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>DNA replication is the synthesis of a new set of DNA by complimentary base pairing. DNA is double stranded right handed helix and the strands are antiparallel. Replication in  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">E</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05764em;">E</span></span></span></span></span>  Coli starts at a unique point called the origin, proceeds in the  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>5</mn><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup><mo>→</mo><msup><mn>3</mn><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5^{\prime} \rightarrow 3^{\prime}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">→</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">3</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  direction in both strands. Replication is semiconservative as each new set of DNA has one old strand and one new strand. Replication at a replicon involves unwinding of DNA, initiation, elongation and several protein (s) factors are involved. Replication in prokaryotes and eukaryotes are similar.</p>
<ol>
<li>A sequence of 245 base pairs in  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">E</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05764em;">E</span></span></span></span></span>  Coli chromosome serves as a starting point for replication. It is designated as Ori C. It has binding sites for DNA binding proteins.</li>
<li>Binding of dna A protein to Ori C initiates unwinding of DNA and synthesis of primer RNA.</li>
<li>A complex of dna B and dna C also binds to Ori C to open the duplex DNA. dna B is helicase. It can bind to single strand DNA. By the action of helicase DNA unwinding occurs.</li>
<li>The unwinding of DNA at ori C causes formation of super coils in DNA molecule which prevents further unwinding of DNA.</li>
<li>DNA Gyrase (only in prokaryotes) and topoisomerase removes super coils by introducing negative super coils to favors replication.</li>
<li>Single strand binding proteins (SSBP) binds to single strands of unwound portion of DNA duplex and stabilizes the single strands. In the absence of SSB, proteins strands can rewind.</li>
<li>Thus by the action of helicase and DNA binding proteins, SSB proteins a replication fork is created. At this fork, both strands of parental DNA serves as templates for the DNA synthesis.</li>
<li>Now to initiate DNA synthesis, a RNA primer is formed by the action of primase, an RNA-polymerase. The length of RNA primers formed is about 10-200 bases. These primers are elongated by DNA polymerase III.</li>
<li>A dimeric DNA-polymerase III holoenzyme synthesizes both strands of DNA at replication fork from RNA primers. Usually one enzyme molecule replicates one strand of DNA.</li>
<li>DNA-polymerase III holoenzyme consist of several subunits. They are  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi><mo separator="true">,</mo><mi>β</mi><mo separator="true">,</mo><mi>γ</mi><mo separator="true">,</mo><mi>δ</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha, \beta, \gamma, \delta</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span><span class="mpunct">,</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05278em;">β</span><span class="mpunct">,</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05556em;">γ</span><span class="mpunct">,</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.03785em;">δ</span></span></span></span></span>  etc.
<span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span>  -Subunit is catalytic subunit. The  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>β</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\beta</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05278em;">β</span></span></span></span></span> -subunit contributes to high processivity. The DNA polymerase III can polymerize hundreds of nucleotides per second whereas DNA polymerase I can polymerize only 10 nucleotides per second.</li>
<li>Since DNA polymerase III cannot polymerize nucleotides in  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>3</mn><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup><mo>→</mo><msup><mn>5</mn><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">3^{\prime} \rightarrow 5^{\prime}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">3</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">→</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  direction, the synthesis of two strands of DNA by this enzyme occurs in some what different manner.</li>
<li>One strand of DNA is synthesized continuously and it is known as leading strand.</li>
<li>Another strand is synthesized discontinuously in the form of okazaki fragments. It is known as lagging strand. The length of these segments ranges from 1000 to 2000 bases. They are also synthesized in  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>5</mn><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup><mo>→</mo><msup><mn>3</mn><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5^{\prime} \rightarrow 3^{\prime}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">→</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">3</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  direction only.</li>
<li>The gaps between okazaki fragments are filled by DNA polymerase I. RNA primers are removed by  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>5</mn><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup><mo>→</mo><msup><mn>3</mn><mo mathvariant="normal" lspace="0em" rspace="0em">′</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">5^{\prime} \rightarrow 3^{\prime}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">5</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">→</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7519em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">3</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7519em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">′</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>  exonuclease activity of DNA polymerase I.</li>
<li>Finally DNA ligase joins the ends of okazaki fragments.
N/B prokaryotes have 3 DNA polymerase as I, II, III. Eukaryotes have many more DNA polymerase and the main one is  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>δ</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\delta</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.03785em;">δ</span></span></span></span></span>  as the main one involved in replication and the others involved in proofreading and repair.</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Proofreading of newly synthesized DNA: It is highly important for the survival of an organism that the nucleotide sequence of DNA be replicated with as few errors as possible. Misreading of the template sequence could result in deleterious, perhaps lethal, mutations. To ensure replication fidelity, DNA polymerase III has, in addition to its 5′→3′ polymerase activity, a “proofreading” activity (3′→5′ exonuclease). As each nucleotide is added to the chain, DNA polymerase III checks to make certain the added nucleotide is, in fact, correctly matched to its complementary base on the template. If it is not, the 3′→5′ exonuclease activity corrects the mistake. Eukaryotes also have a similar proofreading mechanism</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="transcription-steps-in-rna-synthesis">TRANSCRIPTION (STEPS IN RNA SYNTHESIS)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#transcription-steps-in-rna-synthesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The process of transcription of a typical gene of E. coli can be divided into three phases: initiation, elongation, and termination. A transcription unit extends from the promoter to the termination region, and the initial product of transcription by RNA polymerase is termed the primary transcript.</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Initiation: Transcription begins with the binding of the RNA polymerase holoenzyme to a region of the DNA known as the promoter, which is not transcribed. The prokaryotic promoter contains characteristic consensus sequences. [Note: Consensus sequences are idealized sequences in which the base shown at each position is the base most frequently (but not necessarily always) encountered at that position.] Those that are recognized by prokaryotic RNA polymerase σ factors include:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Elongation: Once the promoter region has been recognized and bound by the holoenzyme, local unwinding of the DNA helix continues, mediated by the polymerase. [Note: Unwinding generates supercoils in the DNA that can be relieved by DNA topoisomerases, RNA polymerase begins to synthesize a transcript of the DNA sequence, and several short pieces of RNA are made and discarded. The elongation phase is said to begin when the transcript (typically starting with a purine) exceeds ten nucleotides in length. Sigma is then released, and the core enzyme is able to leave (“clear”) the promoter and move along the template strand in a processive manner. During transcription, a short DNA-RNA hybrid helix is formed. Like DNA polymerase, RNA polymerase uses nucleoside triphosphates as substrates and releases pyrophosphate each time a nucleoside monophosphate is added to the growing chain. As with replication, transcription is always in the 5′→3′ direction. In contrast to DNA polymerase, RNA polymerase does not require a primer and does not appear to have proofreading activity.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Termination: The elongation of the single-stranded RNA chain continues until a termination signal is reached. Termination can be intrinsic (spontaneous) or dependent upon the participation of a protein known as the ρ (rho) factor.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>NB- prokaryotes have 1 RNA polymerase that synthesise all types of RNA but eukaryotes have 3 RNA polymerase that synthesise the different types of RNA e.g. RNA polymerase 2 synthesises mRNA and is inhibited by α-amanitin a mashroom poison which leads to liver failure and death. Reverse transcriptase is an enzyme found in RNA viruses like retroviruses that synthesizes DNA from RNA before the DNA is replicated.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="processing-of-eukaryotic-mrna">PROCESSING OF EUKARYOTIC MRNA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#processing-of-eukaryotic-mrna" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Messenger RNA- It is synthesized in large precursor form known as heterogenous nuclear RNA or hnRNA.</p>
<p>Transport of mRNA into cytosol- Newly synthesized mRNA combines with protein to form mRNA-protein complex (mRNP) which come out through nuclear pore by simple diffusion. The post transcriptional modifications it undergo are</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Capping at 5′ end- GDP is added at 5′ end and it is methylated. Methylation occurs in cytosol. RNA Triphosphatase is an essential mRNA processing enzyme in mammals. It is involved in 5′ cap formation. It hydrolyzes terminal γ-phosphate of nascent pre-mRNA to form diphosphate, which is capped with GMP by the enzyme RNA guanylyl transferase. Later GMP under goes methylation.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Poly adenylation at 3′ end- Poly A tail is attached at 3′ end. Poly (A) polymerase (PAP) is responsible for the addition of poly A tail at 3′ end of pre-mRNA.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Splicing- Parts of hnRNA does not contain any genetic information. These unwanted or intervening sequences are known as introns. Parts of hnRNA which contain (carry) genetic information are known as exons. Splicing removes introns and joins exons to form functional mRNA. Splicing requires special proteins and small nuclear RNA’s or SnRNAs. Usually SnRNAs consist of about 100 nucleotides. The base composition of SnRNA is complementary to</p>
</li>
</ol>

<p>the ends of introns. This helps SnRNA and introns to base pair and this leads to loop formation. As a result, adjacent exons come together. Finally removal of intron loop and joining of exons takes place.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="biomolecules">BIOMOLECULES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#biomolecules" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="carbohydrates">CARBOHYDRATES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#carbohydrates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Carbohydrates are the most abundant bio-macro-molecules on the earth. They are commonly known as sugars because most of them have a sweet taste. Chemically all carbohydrates are polyhydroxy (contain many hydroxyl, -OH, groups) aldehydes or ketones. All carbohydrates are hydrates of carbon and they contain C, H and O. The ratio of hydrogen and oxygen in the majority of carbohydrates will be in 2:1 as in water. Some carbohydrates also contain nitrogen, phosphorous and sulfur. Majority of carbohydrates, not all, have the empirical formula  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\mathrm{CH}_2\mathrm{O})_{\mathrm{n}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">CH</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">O</span><span class="mclose"><span class="mclose">)</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.1514em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mathrm mtight">n</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> . In biochemistry, carbohydrates are denoted as saccharides. Carbohydrates are classified into three major categories based on the number of monomer units and the degree of their polymerization. They are:</p>
<p>Monosaccharides- are the basic unit of carbohydrates. They are the fundamental unit of carbohydrates and they cannot be further hydrolyzed to yield smaller carbohydrate units. All monosaccharides consist of a single polyhydroxy aldehyde or ketone unit.</p>
<p>Sugars having aldehyde group are called aldoses and sugars with keto group are called ketoses. Depending on the number of carbon atoms monosaccharides are named as triose (C3), tetrose (C4), pentose (C5), hexose (C6), heptose (C7) and so on. Monosaccharides with five or more carbons mostly exist in cyclic form and using the Haworth projection they can form a furan a 5 member ring or pyran a six membered ring</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-38730029.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg"></p>
<p>Sterioisomers Compounds having same structural formula but differing in spatial configuration are known as sterioisomers. the spatial arrangements of H and OH groups are important, since they contain asymmetric carbon atoms. Asymmetric carbon means four different groups are attached to the same carbon. The presence of asymmetrical carbon atom causes optical activity. When a beam of plane polarized light is passed through a solution of carbohydrates, it will rotate the light either to right or to left. Depending on the rotation, molecules are called dextrorotatory (+) (d) or levorotatory (-). Enantiomers are optical isomers that are superimposable mirror images of each other. They are the D and L isomers. When the furthest asymmetric carbon OH is on the right it forms the D-isomer with reference to D-glyceraldehyde. In clinical practice dextrose is D-glucose and vitamin C is L-ascorbic acid. enzymes are stereospecific and will synthesize one stereoisomer. In some drugs one stereoisomer is effective and the other less effective or even toxic.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-49854b1b.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg"></p>
<p>The drug albuterol contains equal amounts of two enantiomers. Only one of them is effective, and the other may be responsible for the occasional unpleasant side-effects associated with the drug (which is used to dilate the bronchi, e.g., during an attack of asthma). The active form can now be synthesized pure, and called levalbuterol — is available by prescription.</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sugar-derivatives">Sugar derivatives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sugar-derivatives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Sugar acids Under mild oxidation conditions the aldehyde group is oxidized to carboxyl group to produce aldonic acid. Ex: Glucose to Gluconic acid When aldehyde group is protected and the molecule is oxidized the last carbon becomes COOH group to produce uronic acid. Ex: Glucose to Glucuronic acid.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-71b45c9b.jpeg" alt="img-12.jpeg"></p>
<p>Sialic Acids- N-acetylmannosamine and Pyruvic Acid combine to form N-Acetylneuraminic acid (NANA) and has a backbone of 9 carbons (6+3). its further derivatives are collectively called Sialic acids. Sialic acids are important sugar components in glycoproteins and glycolipids.</p>
<p>Sugar alcohols- When treated with reducing agents such as sodium amalgam, hydrogen can reduce sugars. Aldose yields corresponding alcohols. But ketose forms two alcohols, because of appearance of a new asymmetric carbon atom. Ex: Glucose forms sorbitol and fructose forms sorbitol and mannitol. Sorbitol used as sweetener for people with diabetes mellitus since it does not increase blood glucose levels.</p>
<p>Sugar amines (amino sugars) - In amino sugars, the -OH group at C2 is replaced by an amino  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mrow><mo>−</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mn>2</mn></msub></mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\mathrm{-NH_2})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">−</span><span class="mord mathrm">N</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mathrm mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>  group. The attached amino group is often acetylated, and are named as N-acetyl-. Ex: Glucose to Glucosamine. N-AcetylGlucosamine (NAG) and N-Acetyl-Muramic acid (NAM) are the building blocks of peptidoglycan (cell wall of bacteria).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-739c80b8.jpeg" alt="img-13.jpeg"></p>
<p>Deoxy sugars- when the OH of a sugar is replaced by a H e.g. 2-deoxyribose is found in DNA.</p>
<p>Glycosides: When the hemi-acatal group of a monosaccharide is condensed with an alcohol or phenol group, it is called glycoside. is a molecule in which a sugar is bound to another functional group via a glycosidic bond. Glycosides play numerous important roles in living organisms. Many plants store chemicals in the form of inactive glycosides. Cardiac glycosides are a diverse family of naturally derived compounds that bind to and inhibit  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">+</mo></msup><mi mathvariant="normal">/</mi><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">K</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">+</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{Na^{+} / K^{+}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.0213em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">N</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">a</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7713em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">+</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathrm">/</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">K</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7713em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">+</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> -ATPase</p>
<p>Oligosaccharides- they contain 2-10 monosaccharides linked by glycosidic bonds. Those with 2 monosaccharides are disaccharides. E.g.</p>
<p>Maltose - 2 glucose molecules linked by  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span> -1,4 glycosidic bonds</p>
<p>Lactose - milk sugar contains galactose and glucose linked by  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>β</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\beta</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05278em;">β</span></span></span></span></span> -1,4 glycosidic bonds</p>
<p>Sucrose- table sugar contains glucose and fructose linked by  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span> -1,2 glycosidic bonds</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-030dba85.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg"></p>
<p>Polysaccharides contain many monosaccharides linked by glycosidic bonds e.g. The more complex 'polysaccharides' consist of numerous monosaccharide units linked by glycosidic bonds. A physiological example is glycogen. Homopolysaccharides contain the same type of monosaccharide in their structure e.g. starch contains only D-glucose while heteropolysaccharides contains 2 or more different monosaccharides in their structure e.g. hemicellulose contains is a heterogeneous polymer composed of many sugars, such as xylose, arabinose, mannose, and galactose, that are C5 and C6 sugars. Polysaccharides can have structural function like cellulose that form the cellwall of plants contains D-glucose linked by  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>β</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\beta</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05278em;">β</span></span></span></span></span> -1,4-glycosidic bonds to form a linear molecule and it is broken down by cellulase enzyme which is lacking in humans but produced by some microorganisms and fungi.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>15</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Starch- They also have storage function which stores energy in the body for example starch is a storage homopolysaccharide of D-glucose found in plants tubers, seeds and leaves. It is stored in starch granules. It has two components amylose which forms about 20% of starch and contains D-glucose linked by α-1,4-glycosidic bonds and is linear. It is more soluble in water, amylopectin forms about 80% of starch and it is branched with α-1,4-bonds on the straight chain and α-1,6 bonds at the branching point. Starch is digested by enzyme amylase in the GIT which breaks the α-1,4-bonds only.</p>
<p>Glycogen - is a storage homopolysaccharide of D-glucose found in animals liver and muscles its structure is similar to amylopectin but more highly branched. Excess glucose in the diet is stored as glycogen and broken down to glucose when required.</p>
<p>Other common polysaccharides are</p>
<p>Inulin (fructans) – it's a polymer of fructose found in certain plants like chicory,</p>
<p>Chitin- forms the cell wall of fungi and in exoskeleton of insects.</p>
<p>Pectins – used as thickening agents in food industry</p>
<p>Glycosaminoglycans – form extracellular matrix of cells.</p>
<p>Peptidoglycans – form the cell wall of bacteria. They can be hydrolysed by lysozyme found in tears and saliva killing gram positive bacteria. Antibiotics like penicillin inhibit synthesis of bacteria cell wall.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="amino-acids-and-proteins">Amino acids and Proteins<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#amino-acids-and-proteins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Proteins are polymers of amino acids linked by glycosidic bonds. There are 20 α-amino acids found in proteins and these are called standard amino acids. The α-amino acids contain a hydrogen, an α-carboxyl group, α-amino group and a side chain all linked to the α-carbon, the general structure of α-amino acids is as follows</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-71697a71.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<p>Amino acids found in proteins are L-amino acids. Amino acids can be written by 3 letter or 1 letter notation e.g. Glycine-Gly-G, and alanine-Ala-A. The 20 standard amino acids differ in terms of R-group. The R group determines the properties of amino acids which can be polar or non-polar, acidic or basic etc. common properties of amino acids</p>
<ol>
<li>Amino acids with acidic R-group include aspartic acid and glutamic acid (has a γ-carboxyl group which can be used for bonding e.g. in clotting factors)</li>
<li>Amino acids with Basic R-group include arginine, lysine and histidine</li>
<li>Amino acids with aromatic R-group include tyrosine, phenylalanine, tryptophan</li>
<li>Amino acids with branched R-group include leucine, isoleucine, lysine</li>
</ol>
<p>Essential (indispensable) amino acids are those the body cannot synthesize and must be provided in the diet while non-essential (dispensable) amino acids are those the body can synthesize and must not be provided in the diet. Of the 20 standard amino acids 10 are essential and 10 are non-essential in humans. Plants can synthesize all the 20 amino acids. Animal protein is a good source of essential amino acids while plants usually lack one or more essential amino acids e.g. cereal proteins are deficient in lysine.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Essential amino acids</th><th>Non essential amino acids</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Arginine</td><td>Glycine</td></tr><tr><td>Histidine</td><td>Alanine</td></tr><tr><td>isoleucine</td><td>Glutamic acid</td></tr><tr><td>leucine</td><td>Glutamine</td></tr><tr><td>Lysine</td><td>Aspartic acid</td></tr><tr><td>Methionine</td><td>Asparagine</td></tr><tr><td>Phenylalanine</td><td>Tyrosine</td></tr><tr><td>Threonine</td><td>Serine</td></tr><tr><td>Tryptophan</td><td>Proline</td></tr><tr><td>Valine</td><td>Cysteine</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Peptides- Peptides contain 2 or more amino acids linked by peptide bonds. Peptides that contain 2 amino acids are dipeptides, those with 3amino acids are tripeptides etc. peptides with many amino acids are called polypeptides.</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-035755f8.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="proteins">Proteins<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#proteins" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Polypeptides with more than 100 amino acids are called proteins. The polypeptides in proteins are folded in a specific form called a conformation. This can be in a ropelike conformation along its axis to give it tensile strength e.g. in fibrous proteins like collagen or they can be folded in a spherical compact structure like in globular proteins e.g. enzymes, antibodies and plasma proteins. Fibrous proteins are usually not soluble in aqueous environment while globular proteins are soluble in aqueous environment. The information for folding of a protein is determined by the primary structure of the protein which is the amino acid sequence (peptides for a particular protein have specific number of amino acids and in a specific order as coded in DNA). The globular structure of a protein is stabilized by weak bonds like H-bonds, van der Waals, ionic bonds (salt bridges), and hydrophobic bonds. Folding of proteins create binding sites called domains.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-d95584b0.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>
<p>The conformation of a protein that is most suitable for its function is called its native conformation, loss of the native conformation of a protein due to unfolding of the polypeptide is called denaturation which leads to loss (complete or partial) of biological function of the protein in some cases precipitation from solution. Denatured proteins are also easier to digest by enzymes. Protein denaturation is caused by denaturing agents like high temperature, change in pH, detergents and chemical agents like acids and bases etc.</p>
<p>Tertiary proteins have a single polypeptide in their structure while quaternary proteins have 2 or more polypeptides linked by mainly non-covalent bonds.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-7b43c7fc.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="lipids">LIPIDS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#lipids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Lipids are a broad group of naturally-occurring water insoluble biomolecules which includes fats, waxes, sterols, fat-soluble vitamins, monoglycerides, diglycerides, phospholipids, and others and are basically soluble in organic solvents. The functions of lipids include storing energy, signaling, and acting as structural components of cell membranes.</p>
<p>Triglycerides (triacyl glycerols)- they contain 3 fatty acids linked to glycerol by ester bonds. The fatty acids can be the same or different. These are neutral fats and form in which fats are stored in the adipose tissue. The fats are triglycerides that are solids at room temperature and oils are liquids at room temperature. Fats contain a higher proportion of saturated fatty acids while oils have a higher proportion of Cis-unsaturated fatty acids which lower the melting point of triglycerides. Triglycerides are also used to make soaps using the saponification reaction which are salts of fatty acids after reaction with strong base like NaOH.</p>
