<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-3321e119.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg"></p>
<p>Fatty acids- a fatty acid is a carboxylic acid with an aliphatic chain (R-COOH), which is either saturated having only single carbon-carbon bonds in their carbon chain or unsaturated having one or more double carbon-carbon bonds in their carbon chain. Fatty acids can also be long chain, medium chain of short chain fatty acids. Fatty acids are named based on the common name like palmitic acid with 16 carbons and is saturated. The essential fatty acids are <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span>-linoleic acid <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>16</mn><mo>:</mo><mn>2</mn><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mrow><mn>9</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>12</mn></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(16:2\Delta^{9,12})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">16</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">:</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.0641em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">2</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">Δ</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">9</span><span class="mpunct mtight">,</span><span class="mord mtight">12</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> an omega 6 fatty acid and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span>-linolenic acid <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>16</mn><mo>:</mo><mn>3</mn><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><mrow><mn>9</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>12</mn><mo separator="true">,</mo><mn>15</mn></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(16:3\Delta^{9,12,15})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">16</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">:</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.0641em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mord">3</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">Δ</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">9</span><span class="mpunct mtight">,</span><span class="mord mtight">12</span><span class="mpunct mtight">,</span><span class="mord mtight">15</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> an omega 3 fatty acid. fish oils are rich in omega 3 fatty acids which help to prevent heart disease and strokes like alpha-linolenic acid (ALA), eicosapentaenoic acid (EPA), and docosahexaenoic acid (DHA). Arachidonic acid is important for synthesis of prostaglandins.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phospholipids">Phospholipids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phospholipids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>phosphoglycerides are esters of only two fatty acids, phosphoric acid and a trifunctional alcohol - glycerol (IUPAC name is 1,2,3-propantriol). They consist of a glycerol unit with fatty acids attached to the first two carbon atoms, while a phosphoric acid unit, esterified with an alcohol molecule (usually an amino alcohol) is attached to the third carbon atom of glycerol.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-16511230.jpeg" alt="img-20.jpeg"></p>
<p>phosphatidylethanolamines or cephalins. Cephalins are found in brain tissue and nerves and also have a role in blood clotting. Phosphoglycerides containing choline as the amino alcohol unit are called phosphatidylcholines or lecithins. Lecithins occur in all living organisms. Like cephalins, they are important constituents of nerve and brain tissue. Egg yolks are especially rich in lecithins. Dipalmitoyl lecithin is an important phosphatidylcholine found in lungs; it is a surface active agent and prevents the adherence of inner surface of the lungs due to surface tension. Respiratory distress syndrome (RDS) in infants is a disorder characterized by the absence of dipalmitoyl lecithin which accounts for <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>20</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">20\%</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8056em; vertical-align: -0.0556em;"></span><span class="mord">20%</span></span></span></span></span> mortality in premature infants. [Note: cardiolipin is antigenic, and is recognized by antibodies raised against Treponema pallidum, the bacterium that causes syphilis.]</p>
<p>Glycolipids - Glycolipids are lipids with a carbohydrate attached by a glycosidic (covalent) bond. Their role is to maintain the stability of the cell membrane and to facilitate cellular recognition, which is crucial to the immune response and in the connections that allow cells to connect to one another to form tissues. Blood group determinants. Glycolipids are found on the surface of all eukaryotic cell membranes, where they extend from the phospholipid bilayer into the extracellular environment. Sphingoglycolipids contain the alcohol sphingosine bound to a fatty acids and a sugar group this are cerebrosides and gangliosides.</p>
<p>Terpenes (Isoprenoids) - derivatives of isoprene which is a hypothetical 5 carbon compound. They form coloured compounds in plants like carotenoids, rubber, e.t.c. the yellow colour of the eggyork contains carotenes which can be converted to vitamin A.</p>
<p>Steroids- they are compounds with a steroid nucleus. Sterols are any steroid-based alcohol having a hydrocarbon (aliphatic) side-chain of 8-10 carbons at the 17-beta position and a hydroxyl group at the 3-beta position (therefore an alcohol).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-40597e00.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg"></p>
<p>They are lipid soluble and commonly found associated with lipids. Cholesterol (a zoosterol) is commonly found in animal tissue. The human body can synthesize its own cholesterol from acetyl CoA so it is not required in the diet. Cholesterol is used to synthesize steroid hormones (e.g. gonad sex hormones like testosterone and adrenal cortex</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>hormones), in cell membrane to control fluidity, synthesis of vitamin D, and synthesis of chemical contraceptives. Plant sterols are phytosterols and mycosterols from fungi. Excess cholesterol in blood is hypercholesterolemia and is associated with coronary heart disease.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nucleotides">Nucleotides<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nucleotides" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nucleotides are the building blocks of nucleic acids. There are 2 types of nucleotides ribonucleotides found in RNA and deoxy-ribonucleotides found in DNA. Nucleotides are made of 3 components</p>
<p>Nitrogenous base- this can be pyrimidines like cytosine (C), thymine (T), uracil (U) or purines which can be Adenine (A) or Guanine (G). d. some plant purines are caffeine of coffee, theophylline of tea, and theobromine of cocoa. Caffeine and theophylline act as CNS stimulants.</p>
<p>Pentose sugar can be ribose found RNA or deoxyribose found in DNA</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-e6282f19.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-2af3cfc1.jpeg" alt="img-23.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phosphate-group-can-be-mono-di-or-triphosphate-with-1-2-or-3-phosphates-respectively-bound-to-carbon-5-of-the-pentose-sugar">Phosphate group can be mono, di or triphosphate with 1,2 or 3 phosphates respectively bound to carbon 5 of the pentose sugar<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phosphate-group-can-be-mono-di-or-triphosphate-with-1-2-or-3-phosphates-respectively-bound-to-carbon-5-of-the-pentose-sugar" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Nucleosides (bottom) are made of a nitrogenous base, usually either a purine or pyrimidine, and a five-carbon carbohydrate ribose</p>
<p>Structure of a nucleotides the numbering of the sugar is in prime form. the base is attached to carbon 1 of the pentose in a beta conformation  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mn>1</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\mathrm{N}_1</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; <div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="functions-of-nucleotides">Functions of nucleotides<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#functions-of-nucleotides" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Building blocks of nucleic acids</li>
<li>High energy molecules e.g. ATP is the energy currency of the cell</li>
<li>Signaling molecules e.g. second messengers like cyclic AMP and cyclic GMP</li>
<li>Form coenzymes e.g.  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><msup><mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi><mo lspace="0em" rspace="0em">+</mo></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{NAD^{+}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7713em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">NA</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">D</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.7713em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">+</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
<li>Regulatory molecules, allosteric regulators AMP</li>
<li>Form activated intermediates e.g. UDP-glucose</li>
<li>Nucleotide and base analogues are used as antiviral and anticancer drugs</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="minerals">MINERALS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#minerals" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are 103 known elements. Living organisms are composed mainly of 11 of these. Namely carbon, hydrogen, oxygen, nitrogen and the seven major minerals:</p>
<ul>
<li>Calcium, phosphorus and magnesium, which are used mainly in bone</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>

<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Sodium, potassium and chloride, which are the main electrolytes present in the intracellular and extracellular fluid</li>
<li>Sulphur, which is used mainly in amino acids. The RNI is greater than 100 mg/day for each of these (the exception is sulphur for which no RNI has been published). In addition, there are at least 12 other elements that are required in the diet in smaller quantities. These are known as the essential trace elements, for which the RNI is less than 100 mg/day: iron, zinc, copper, cobalt, iodine, chromium, manganese, molybdenum, selenium, vanadium, nickel and silicon.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="calcium">CALCIUM<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#calcium" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Calcium is the most abundant mineral in the human body. There is about 1.2 kg of calcium in the average 70-kg adult, of which 99% is in bone. RNI- The RNI of calcium is 700 mg/day; it is higher during periods of growth, pregnancy, lactation and after the menopause.</p>
<p><strong>Sources</strong>- Milk and milk products; a lot of foods are fortified with calcium, for example, bread.</p>
<p><strong>Absorption</strong>- Absorption of calcium from the diet is variable depending on the following factors:</p>
<ul>
<li>Lactose and basic amino acids increase absorption because they form complexes with calcium</li>
<li>Fibre decreases absorption, therefore, vegans need a lot more calcium.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Active forms</strong>- The ionized form, Ca²⁺</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="main-functions">Main functions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#main-functions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The main functions of calcium are listed</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Fig. 11.1 Main functions of calcium.</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Function</td><td>Examples</td></tr><tr><td>Structural role</td><td>Bone and teeth</td></tr><tr><td>Calcium is present as calcium phosphate (hydroxyapatite) crystals</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Muscle contraction</td><td>Calcium binds to troponin C</td></tr><tr><td>Nerve impulse transmission</td><td>Calcium is released in response to hormones and neurotransmitters</td></tr><tr><td>Blood clotting</td><td>Coenzyme for coagulation factors</td></tr><tr><td>Ion transport and cell signalling</td><td>Intracellular second messenger</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><strong>Regulation of calcium</strong>- Calcium levels are controlled by three hormones which also regulate plasma phosphate levels:</p>
<ul>
<li>Parathyroid hormone, which increases plasma calcium but decreases levels of inorganic phosphate</li>
<li>Vitamin D, which increases both plasma calcium and inorganic phosphate levels</li>
<li>Calcitonin, which decreases both plasma calcium and inorganic phosphate levels.</li>
</ul>
<p>Calcium deficiency- In children, calcium deficiency causes rickets (derived from the old English word 'wrickken' meaning to twist). In adults, calcium deficiency causes osteomalacia. They both may occur:</p>
<ul>
<li>From dietary deficiency of calcium, particularly in developing countries</li>
<li>Secondary to vitamin D deficiency. Vitamin D is necessary for the intestinal absorption of calcium and phosphate</li>
<li>From malabsorption (e.g. coeliac disease).</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Calcium overload: hypercalcaemia</strong>- Causes- Major causes of hypercalcaemia are primary hyperparathyroidism and malignant disease and have nothing to do with nutrition, and are therefore, beyond the scope of this book. Very rarely, hypercalcaemia is associated with the excessive ingestion of milk and antacids for the control of indigestion. This decreases the renal excretion of calcium: milk-alkali syndrome.</p>
<p><strong>Clinical features</strong>- Calcium ions are normally found in cells and deposited calcium salts are present in bones and teeth. In overload, calcium salts are deposited in normal tissues, leading to tissue metastatic calcification and impaired function. This may cause renal stones, arrhythmias, heart failure and calcification of the arteries. Muscle weakness, tiredness, anorexia, constipation and a sluggish, nervous response may also be seen</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phosphorus-rni-550-mg-day">PHOSPHORUS- RNI- 550 mg/day<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phosphorus-rni-550-mg-day" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Sources</strong>- Most foods; a dietary deficiency has not been described. Functions</p>
<p>Phosphorus works in conjunction with vitamin D and calcium:</p>
<ul>
<li>It has a structural role in bones and teeth</li>
<li>It is required for the production of ATP and other phosphorylated metabolic intermediates. Therefore, it is fundamental to the maintenance of function of cells in the body.</li>
</ul>
<p>Deficiency and toxicity</p>
<p>The clinical manifestations of a deficiency and excess of phosphorus are listed in Fig. 11.6.</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Fig. 11.6 The effects of phosphorous deficiency and excess.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Deficiency</th><th>Excess</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>If severe (&lt;0.3 mmol/L), will affect the function of all cells causing:</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• muscle weakness</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• in erythrocytes leads to a decrease information of 2,3-bisphosphoglycerate and therefore, reduces unloading of oxygen to tissues</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• rickets and osteomalacia</td><td>May combine with calcium to produce calcium phosphate and be deposited in tissues</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>MAGNESIUM- RNI- 270 mg/day. Sources- Most foods, especially green vegetables.</p>
<p>Functions- The functions of magnesium are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Structural role in bones and teeth</li>
<li>Cofactor for more than 300 enzymes in the body, that is, those enzymes that catalyse ATP- dependent reactions. Magnesium binds to ATP, forming a magnesium-ATP complex which is the substrate for enzymes such as kinases</li>
<li>Interacts with calcium to affect the permeability of excitable membranes and neuromuscular transmission.</li>
</ul>
<p>Deficiency- Seen in alcoholics; patients with liver cirrhosis; following diuretic therapy; and in renal and intestinal disease. The symptoms are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Muscle weakness</li>
<li>Secondary calcium deficiency</li>
<li>Confusion, hallucinations, convulsions and other neurological symptoms</li>
<li>Can also lead to hypokalaemia.</li>
</ul>
<p>Excess- Extremely rare.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sodium-potassium-and-chloride">SODIUM, POTASSIUM AND CHLORIDE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sodium-potassium-and-chloride" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Sodium, potassium and chloride function together to regulate the osmolality of intracellular and extracellular fluids. Sodium is the most important one in this respect. Importantly, both high and low potassium concentration in plasma can cause severe cardiac problems</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Solution</th><th>pH</th><th>Na⁺</th><th>Cl⁻</th><th>K⁺</th><th>Ca²⁺</th><th>Lactate</th><th>Glucose</th><th>Osmolality (Osm)</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>0.9% 'normal' saline</td><td>5.0</td><td>154</td><td>154</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>308</td></tr><tr><td>Hartmann's solution/ compound sodium lactate</td><td>6.5</td><td>30</td><td>109</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>28</td><td>0</td><td>275</td></tr><tr><td>% Dextrose</td><td>4.0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>50 g/L</td><td>252</td></tr></tbody></table>

causes of loss include: vomiting, diarrhoea, use of diuretics, Addison's disease, hyperglycaemia (causing an osmotic diuresk) or renal failure</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• sodium loss is usually accompanied by water loss, leading to decrease in plasma volume and signs of circulatory failure and collapse</td><td>• loss may be secondary to vomiting and the use of diuretics, diarrhoea, excess steroids, hyperaldosteronism (e.g. Conn's syndrome), Cushing's syndrome or alkalosis</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• high chance of cardiac arrhythmias and neuromuscular weakness</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• remember, intravenous insulin treatment without supplementation of potassium leads to hypokalaemia</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Excess</td><td>Role in hypertension: sodium overload linked to water overload can lead to oedema and to cardiac failure</td><td>• the most common cause of potassium retention and hyperkalaemia is renal failure</td></tr><tr><td>• both severe hypokalaemia and hyperkalaemia are dangerous and require immediate treatment</td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Fluid and electrolyte balance management is an important aspect of medicine, and rarely well understood. Key intravenous fluids you may prescribe for patients include 3 main classes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Crystalloids (0.9% Saline, 5% Dextrose (glucose)) and on rarer occasions Hartmann's</li>
<li>Colloids (gelofusine, albumin)</li>
<li>Blood and blood products.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>21</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The choice depends on whether you wish to replete the circulatory volume, top up electrolytes or maintain fluid balance. The fluids all have different compositions of electrolytes and should be used with caution in certain groups of patients. Key composition of commonly prescribed crystalloids:</p>
<p>It is important to note that Hartmann's solution is the most physiological of the solutions with a composition and osmolality most similar to plasma values (normal plasmaOsm: 275–299 milli-osmoles per kilogram). Also note that 0.9% Saline is often referred to as 'normal saline'; please remember this is not a 'normal' fluid as the name implies, and as with all fluids, it should be only prescribed once existing electrolyte and fluid statuses are known.</p>
<p>Hyperkalaemia is a medical emergency and requires immediate attention, especially when the potassium concentration is above 6 mmol/l or there are associated ECG changes. Refer to local and current guidelines when managing, focus on the protection of the cardiac membrane by giving calcium gluconate intravenously.</p>
<p>This buys one time to treat the hyperkalaemia by giving infusion of both insulin and glucose to shift potassium ions into the cell, lowering the plasma potassium concentration.</p>
<p>Hypokalaemia (&lt;2.5 mmol/L) is also a medical emergency that can result in cardiac arrhythmias and muscle weakness. This should be managed with potassium replacement.</p>
<p>IRON- RNI- The daily loss of iron from the body is 0.5–1.0 mg/day and is due to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Gastrointestinal tract turnover, about 0.5 mg/day</li>
<li>Desquamation of intestinal mucosal cells and biliary excretion, about 0.3 mg/day</li>
<li>Sweat and desquamation of skin cells, about 0.1 mg/day</li>
<li>Urinary losses, about 0.1 mg/day.</li>
</ul>
<p>Small daily losses are accounted for by the absorption of dietary iron in the duodenum. The demand for iron increases during growth, pregnancy and menstruation (1 ml of blood loss is equal to 0.5 mg of iron). The daily iron requirements are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Adult male 1.0 mg</li>
<li>Child 1.5 mg</li>
<li>Menstruating woman 2.0 mg</li>
<li>Pregnant woman 3.0 mg</li>
</ul>
<p>Only 10% of dietary iron is absorbed, therefore, the amount ingested daily is equal to the daily requirement 10. Therefore, the RNI 410–20 mg/day.</p>
<p>Sources- Liver, meat, green vegetables and cereals. Dietary iron exists in two forms:</p>
<ul>
<li>Haem iron, which is derived from haemoglobin or myoglobin in meat and is rapidly absorbed</li>
<li>Non-haem iron, which is present in vegetables and cereals and is absorbed slowly.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="absorption-transport-and-storage">Absorption, transport and storage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#absorption-transport-and-storage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A summary of the absorption, transport, and functions of iron is given in Fig. 11.8. Total body iron is about 3–5 g. About 60% is in haemoglobin and most of the rest is stored mainly as ferritin, which is a protein–iron complex. A small amount of iron is also stored as haemosiderin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="zinc">ZINC<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#zinc" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The daily zinc requirement is 2–3 mg/day but absorption is only about 30% effective. Therefore, the RNI is 10 mg/day. Zinc can be found in most foods. The total body zinc is 2–3 g. It is found in all tissues but high concentrations are present in the liver, kidney, bone, retina, muscles and prostate. The role of zinc in the body is described in</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Fig. 11.12 The role of zinc in the body.</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Functions</td><td>Deficiency</td></tr><tr><td>Co-factor of over 100 enzymes, e.g.:</td><td>Causes: growth retardation, hypogonadism, and delayed wound healing</td></tr><tr><td>dehydrogenases, e.g. LDH</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>peptidases</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>carbonic anhydrase</td><td>These effects are mainly a result of decreased activity of the enzymes of DNA synthesis</td></tr><tr><td>enzymes of DNA and protein synthesis</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>superoxide dismutase</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Transcription factors contain 'zinc fingers' that enable them to bind DNA</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>COPPER – RNI 1.2 mg/day. Sources- Liver is a very good source.</p>
<p>Copper metabolism- The total body copper is about 75–150 mg. High copper concentrations are found in the liver, brain, heart and kidneys. Dietary copper is absorbed in the stomach and duodenum and transported to the liver loosely bound to albumin; the absorption is about 30% effective. It is incorporated into caeruloplasmin, a</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>glycoprotein synthesized by the liver, which transports copper to the tissues where it can be used for the synthesis of other copper-containing enzymes. Normally, it is excreted in the bile (daily loss is approximately 2–3 mg/day). In the blood, 80–90% of the copper present is bound to caeruloplasmin.</p>
<p><strong>Function</strong>- Copper is required for the synthesis of a number of copper-containing enzymes (Fig. 11.13). Copper deficiency: Menkes’ kinky hair syndrome Menkes’ kinky hair syndrome is a rare, X-linked disease with an incidence of 1 in 50000–100000. It is caused by the defective absorption of copper from the intestine, leading to a decreased synthesis of copper-containing enzymes</p>

limbus</td><td></td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><strong>Treatment</strong>- Copper therapy has no significant effect. The life expectancy is less than 2 years.</p>
<p><strong>Copper overload</strong>: Wilson’s disease</p>
<p><strong>Treatment</strong>- Wilson’s disease is treated by daily chelation therapy with D-penicillamine. This is very effective at binding copper and eliminating it in the urine. However, the resulting liver and neurological damage is permanent.</p>
<p><strong>Iodine</strong>- The human body contains about 15–20 mg of iodine, most of which is in the thyroid gland. It is essential for the synthesis of the thyroid hormones thyroxine and triiodothyronine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="deficiency">Deficiency<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#deficiency" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Endemic goiter</strong>- Endemic goitre, a generalized enlargement of the thyroid gland, occurs in areas where the soil and water lack iodine, such that the daily intake is less than 70 mg (usually mountainous areas). In the UK, it used to be found in people in Derbyshire, causing the so-called Derbyshire neck. The problem has now been eliminated in most countries by the addition of iodine to table salt and its prevalence is mostly restricted to developing countries.</p>
<p><strong>Pathogenesis of goiter</strong>- Normally iodine is used to make thyroxine and triiodothyronine.</p>
<p>Increased levels of these hormones exert a negative feedback effect on the hypothalamus and anterior pituitary inhibiting further release of thyroid releasing hormone and thyroid-stimulating hormone, resulting in a decrease in their synthesis.</p>
<p>However, low levels of iodine decrease thyroxine formation by the thyroid gland. This releases the negative feedback on the hypothalamic-pituitary axis, causing an uncontrolled increase in thyroid-stimulating hormone secretion. High levels of TSH overstimulate the thyroid gland, causing hyperplasia of the thyroid epithelium and generalized enlargement</p>
<p>The addition of iodine to the diet should reverse this effect.</p>
<p><strong>Cretinism</strong>- Pregnant mothers who are deficient in iodine may give birth to babies who are hypothyroid. Growth and mental development in these babies are severely impaired and may be irreversible. The diagnosis is made by the neonatal screening test, the Guthrie test, which is performed on all newborn babies and looks for raised thyroid-stimulating hormone levels. The same sample is used to screen for phenylketonuria.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>23</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Treatment- is lifelong oral replacement of thyroxine. Cretinism can be prevented by the iodination of salt in the maternal diet.</p>
<p>Clinical Note- Hypothyroidism has an insidious onset with vague, non-specific and diverse features. It usually presents with fatigue, weight gain and cold intolerance. It may also present with bradycardia, constipation, delayed puberty, growth and mental retardation, and dry, scaly, cold and thickened skin.</p>
<p>Iodine overload- Excessive dietary iodine may cause the symptoms of hyperthyroidism, that is, an overactive thyroid.</p>

<p>Water  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mi mathvariant="bold">H</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mi mathvariant="bold">O</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\mathbf{H}_2\mathbf{O})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathbf">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mord mathbf">O</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>  - Water molecules contain 2 hydrogens linked to one oxygen. The hydrogens have a partial positive charge and the oxygen a partially negative charge so water is a polar solvent. Water has a neutral pH of 7. Water form hydrogen bonds, (weak interactions that form between a hydrogen with a partial positive charge and a more electronegative atom, such as oxygen. The hydrogen atoms involved in hydrogen bonding must be attached to electronegative atoms, such as NNstart text, N, or, F. A charged or polar substance that interacts with and dissolves in</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>water is said to be <strong>hydrophilic</strong>. In contrast, nonpolar molecules like oils and fats do not interact well with water. They separate from it rather than dissolve in it and are called <strong>hydrophobic</strong>.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-066d462b.jpeg" alt="img-25.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-c4900b53.jpeg" alt="img-26.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-88c761dc.jpeg" alt="img-27.jpeg"></p>
<p>If you stir table salt into water, the crystal lattice of NaCl will begin to dissociate into Na++start superscript, plus, end superscript and Cl—start superscript, minus, end superscript ions. (Dissociation is just a name for the process in which a compound or molecule breaks apart to form ions.) Water dissolves ionic compounds like salts by hydration where the partially positive hydrogens surround the cation and the partially -ve oxygen surround the anion. When there are many water molecules relative to solute molecules, as in an aqueous solution, these interactions lead to the formation of a three-dimensional sphere of water molecules, or hydration shell, around the solute. Hydration shells allow particles to be dispersed (spread out) evenly in water. As the process continues, all of the ions in the table salt crystals are surrounded by hydration shells and dispersed in solution. Water can stick to itself (cohesion) and other molecules (adhesion). Water has a high heat capacity and heat of vaporization, and ice—solid water—is less dense than liquid water.</p>
<p>Water owes these unique properties to the polarity of its molecules and, specifically, to their ability to form hydrogen bonds with each other and with other molecules. Organic molecules that are uncharged but have functional groups that can form hydrogen bonds with water are also soluble in water e.g. sugars, alcohols, etc.</p>
<p>Nonpolar molecules, like fats and oils, don't interact with water or form hydration shells. These molecules don't have regions of partial positive or partial negative charge, so they aren't electrostatically attracted to water molecules. Thus, rather than dissolving, nonpolar substances (such as oils) stay separate and form layers or droplets when added to water.</p>
<p><strong>Osmosis</strong> - Osmosis is the movement of water across a semi-permeable membrane from an area of low solute concentration to an area of high solute concentration. Osmosis helps regulate the flow of water in and out of cells. For example, when comparing two solutions that have different osmolarities, the solution with the higher osmolarity is said to be <strong>hyperosmotic</strong> to the other, and the solution with lower osmolarity is said to be <strong>hypoosmotic</strong>. If two solutions have the same osmolarity, they are said to be <strong>isoosmotic</strong>.</p>
<p>The ability of an extracellular solution to make water move into or out of a cell by osmosis is known as its <strong>tonicity</strong>. If a cell is placed in a <strong>hypertonic</strong> solution, there will be a net flow of water out of the cell, and the cell will lose volume. A solution will be hypertonic to a cell if its solute concentration is higher than that inside the cell, and the solutes cannot cross the membrane.</p>
<p>If a cell is placed in a <strong>hypotonic</strong> solution, there will be a net flow of water into the cell, and the cell will gain volume. If the solute concentration outside the cell is lower than inside the cell, and the solutes cannot cross the membrane, then that solution is hypotonic to the cell.</p>
<p>If a cell is placed in an <strong>isotonic</strong> solution, there will be no net flow of water into or out of the cell, and the cell's volume will remain stable. If the solute concentration outside the cell is the same as inside the cell, and the solutes cannot cross the membrane, then that solution is isotonic to the cell.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-d6e6f89c.jpeg" alt="img-28.jpeg">

<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-efb7a6b7.jpeg" alt="img-29.jpeg">

normal cells

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="unit-2-metabolism">UNIT 2 METABOLISM<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-2-metabolism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>ENZYMES- Enzymes are proteins that act as biological catalysts by accelerating chemical reactions. The molecules upon which enzymes may act are called substrates, and the enzyme converts the substrates into products. Almost all metabolic processes in the cell need enzyme catalysis in order to occur at rates fast enough to sustain life. Metabolic pathways depend upon enzymes to catalyze individual steps. The study of enzymes is called enzymology. Other biocatalysts are catalytic RNA molecules, called ribozymes. Enzymes that work inside the cells that synthesise them are called intracellular enzymes and those which work outside the cells that synthesize it are extracellular enzymes.</p>
<p>Constitutive enzymes are These enzymes are produced in constant amounts without regard to the physiological demand or the concentration of the substrate. Inducible enzyme one whose production requires or is stimulated by a specific small molecule, the inducer, which is the substrate of the Enzymes or of a substance resembling its substrate. Enzymes can regulate metabolic pathways and these enzymes are regulatory enzymes which are usually allosteric enzymes. In some genetic disorders, there may be a deficiency one or several enzymes (E.g. albinism). According to IUPAC enzymes have a common name which we use for day to day use like trypsin and a systematic name based on the reaction they catalyse. The systematic name removes confusion and enzymes are classified into 7 classes and an EC: number like EC:2,3,4,1 where the first number is the class, second the subclass, third sub-sub-class and the fourth the serial number of the enzyme.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-are-the-properties-of-enzymes">What are the Properties of Enzymes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-are-the-properties-of-enzymes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Enzymes are proteins in nature and they are affected by denaturing agents and inhibitors</li>
<li>Catalytic Property- The enzymes have extraordinary catalytic power. Enzymes have extra-ordinary (superfast) catalytic power. They are active in very small quantities therefore A small amount of enzyme is enough to convert a large quantity of substrates. Enzymes accelerate reactions up to <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>10</mn><mn>14</mn></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10^{14}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">14</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> to <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>10</mn><mn>20</mn></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10^{20}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">20</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> times. Turn over number: The number of substrate molecules converted by one molecule of enzyme per second when its active site is saturated with the substrate.</li>
<li>They function in an aqueous solution The enzymes remain unchanged after the reaction so can bind to other substrates</li>
<li>Catalysts are not required in stoichiometric quantities</li>
<li>Enzymes have a high degree of specificity for their substrates and reactions. They can be absolute specificity where it acts on only 1 substrate, group specificity- the enzyme works on a group of related substrates, stereospecificity- acts on a specific stereoisomer and reaction specificity- catalyses specific reaction</li>
<li>Enzymes do not change the equilibrium of reaction</li>
<li>Most of the enzymes catalyzed reactions are reversible. The reversibility of the reaction depends upon the requirements of the cell. In some cases, there are separate enzymes for forward and reverse reaction</li>
</ul>
<p>Structures of enzymes - Enzymes are globular proteins and they may be tertiary or quaternary protein. They are larger than their substrate and the substrate binds to a specific site called the active site. The active site has a substrate binding site and the catalytic site. The active site is a hydrophobic cleft on the enzyme lined by amino acids required for substrate binding and catalysis. The active site has specific symmetry that is responsible for its specificity. Even though most of the enzymes contain thousands of amino acids only 2-4 amino acids are directly involved in the catalysis. Allosteric site: Additional sites other than the active site for the binding of allosteric modulators. Allosteric modulators are involved in the regulation of enzymatic activity and can be activators or inhibitors. Simple enzymes contain amino acids only in their active form while complex proteins contain a cofactor to be active. The protein part alone (apoenzyme) is inactive and combined to the cofactor forms the holoenzyme (active).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-e2910fb9.jpeg" alt="img-31.jpeg"></p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Cofactors can be metal ions like <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{Mg}^{2+}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.0818em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm" style="margin-right: 0.01389em;">Mg</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8873em;"><span style="top: -3.1362em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span><span class="mord mtight">+</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> which stabilize the protein structure of involved in electron transfer and coenzymes which are small organic compounds mainly derived from water soluble vitamins. The body converts them to coenzymes e.g. folic acid is converted to tetrahydrofolate (THF). They are involved to electron transfer or transfer of functional group.</p>
<p><strong>Mode of action of enzymes</strong>- the enzyme must bind to the substrate at the active site to form an enzyme substrate complex before it is converted to product. Enzymes find an alternative pathway in converting substrate to product. Enzyme reduces the activation energy of the reaction. The minimum energy that must be input to a chemical system, containing potential reactants, in order for a chemical reaction to occur. For a chemical reaction to proceed at a reasonable rate, there should exist an appreciable number of molecules with energy equal to or greater than the activation energy. The activation energy of a reaction is denoted as <strong>Ea</strong>.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-758c990c.jpeg" alt="img-32.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="factors-affecting-enzyme-action">Factors affecting enzyme action<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-affecting-enzyme-action" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Temperature</strong>- enzymes work best at the optimum temperature. Low temperatures below optimum deactivate the enzyme and high temperatures above optimum denature the enzymes. In humans the optimum temperature is close to normal body temperature. Enzymes are very sensitive to heat and temperature. (They are <strong>thermo-labile</strong>.). At higher temperature (60-70°C), the enzyme is destroyed or denatured. Do you know an enzyme active at very high temperature? It is Taq-Polymerase used in PCR reactions. The optimum temperature for Taq-polymerase is 75 to 80°C.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-74d0df70.jpeg" alt="img-33.jpeg"></p>
<p><strong>pH</strong>- Some enzymes are active in acidic pH (pH below 7), some are active in basic pH (pH above 7) and some others are in neutral pH depending at their site of action. Although some enzymes exhibit activity in a broad range of pH, many show <strong>maximal activity</strong> at a narrow pH range [4–6], referred to as the pH optimum</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-2743fc43.jpeg" alt="img-34.jpeg"></p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>27</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Inhibitors- it is a substance that slows down or blocks the catalytic activity of an enzyme. inhibitors are molecules that decrease enzyme activity, and activators are molecules that increase activity. Many therapeutic drugs and poisons are enzyme inhibitors. Inhibitors can bind reversibly of irreversibly. Irreversible inhibitors bind covalently to the enzyme and destroy it. Reversible inhibitors bind reversibly with the enzyme. They include competitive inhibitors which compete with the natural substrate of the enzyme for the active site and binds only to the free enzyme. It can be overcome by increasing concentration of the substrate. Many drugs are competitive inhibitors e.g. allopurinol acts as a competitive inhibitor of xanthine oxidase. Non-competitive inhibitors- the inhibitor binds at an allosteric site separate from the active site of substrate binding and prevent the conversion of substrate to product. They bind to free enzyme or the enzyme substrate complex.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="enzyme-regulation">Enzyme regulation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#enzyme-regulation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Feedback inhibition- Feedback inhibition is a specific type of allosteric enzymatic activity regulation mechanism in cells. In some multi-enzyme pathways, the regulatory enzyme is specifically inhibited by the end product of the pathway whenever the concentration of the end product exceeds the cell's requirements. This type of regulation by the end product inhibiting to the first enzyme of the multi-enzymatic pathway is called feedback inhibition. Example of allosteric feedback inhibition is the biosynthesis of L-isoleucine from L-threonine in bacteria</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-0a1ec154.jpeg" alt="img-35.jpeg"></p>
<p>Feedback regulation- A type of enzymatic activity regulation. Here the end product of an enzymatic pathway directly inhibit the synthesis of concerned enzyme by interfering with the gene of that enzyme. Enzyme is not directly inhibited by the end product. End product reduces the concentration of enzyme by inhibiting the synthesis of new enzyme molecules. Best example is the reduction of HMG CoA reductase enzyme by dietary cholesterol which is the key rate limiting enzyme in cholesterol synthesis.</p>
<p>Isoenzymes- Isoenzymes (also known as isozymes) are enzymes that differ in amino acid sequence but catalyze the same chemical reaction. Believed to be originating from closely linked genes or from multiple gene loci. Evolution from a single form possibly due to long-term mutations. They vary with respect to their kinetic parameters, electrophoretic mobility, and localization. They all have independent action. E.g. Lactate dehydrogenase have 5 isoenzymes (LDH1, LDH2, LDH3, LDH4 &amp; LDH5). They can be used to identify the specific affected tissues. They can be differentiated from each other and can be clinically quantified in the lab. Phosphodiesterase enzymes are found in a number of isoenzymes inhibited by different substances and found in different tissues in the body.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="metabolism-of-biomolecules">Metabolism of biomolecules<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#metabolism-of-biomolecules" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Respiration- reactions of the cell involved in energy production mainly as ATP. The cell will mostly use glucose as the source of Energy. When glucose supply is limited in the body the cell can use fats or proteins during starvation. Glucose enters the cell using facilitated diffusion using glut transporters (1-5) which have different specificities. GLUT 5 transports fructose.</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>GLUT transporter</th><th>Key feature</th><th>Location</th><th>Reason</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>GLUT-1</td><td>Low Km, so highly active</td><td>All cells</td><td>Regulates basal uptake even at low glucose levels, ensures a constant supply of energy for survival</td></tr><tr><td>GLUT-2</td><td>Concentration-dependent, due to high Km</td><td>Liver, pancreatic islets</td><td>Acts as a glucose sensor – increases uptake in high glucose levels for storage. <p>Glycolysis – is the oxidation of glucose to 2 pyruvate. It takes place in the cytosol of the cell. It occurs in 10 steps. Phases of Glycolysis- Glycolysis can be considered as a two part process. Firstly, energy is consumed to generate high energy intermediates, which then go on to release their energy during the second phase.</p>
<ul>
<li>Energy investment phase – requires two ATP molecules to produce high energy intermediates.</li>
<li>Energy pay out phase – The intermediate is metabolized, producing four ATP molecules and two NADH molecules. it is important to note the following:</li>
</ul>
<ol>
<li>The net effect is that 2 ATP and 2 NADH are produced.</li>
<li>Reactions 1, 3 and 10 are unidirectional and are therefore key regulatory steps.</li>
<li>Molecules are able to enter glycolysis mid-way through the pathway via the intermediates produced.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-1b63e712.jpeg" alt="img-36.jpeg"></p>
<p>Hexokinase is common in most cells in the body, it acts non glucose and other hexases and has high affinity for glucose while glucokinase is found in the liver, <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>β</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\beta</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05278em;">β</span></span></span></span></span>-cells of pancreas and kidney. It has low affinity for glucose and specific for glucose, its inducible.</p>

<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Inhibitors of glycolysis- Sodium fluoride has antiglycolytic effect that inhibits glycolysis by erythrocytes in blood collected for glucose analysis to prevent lower readings of blood glucose, other inhibitors are lodoacetate and arsenate inhibit the enzyme glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate dehydrogenase</p>
<ol>
<li>Anaerobic respiration takes place in tissues which are deficient of oxygen where in animals pyruvate id converted to L-lactic acid. The lactic acid is formed in muscles during strenuous exercise which causes muscle pain and fatigue. Accumulation of lactic acid in blood causes lactic acidosis. In yeast Pyruvate is converted to acetaldehyde and then to ethanol. In bacteria pyruvate is converted to other organic acids like D-lactic acid, propionic acid, acetic acid, butanoic acid etc.</li>
</ol>
<p>In situations where cells' oxygen demands increase above supply (i.e. ischaemia), glycolysis will quickly occur, producing lactic acid. This may occur physiologically, such as in the muscles during intense exercise, or pathologically, for example in ischaemic heart disease or when a malignant tumour outgrows its blood supply.</p>
<p>Removal of lactate- The lactate produced as a result of anaerobic respiration must be removed from the blood as it is acidic. There are two main ways to do this:</p>
<ul>
<li>Lactate is transported to metabolically active cells, such as in the heart and brain. Here it is converted back to pyruvate, which is then utilized in the Krebs cycle.</li>
<li>Lactate is transported to the liver and converted to pyruvate. Pyruvate is then used in the process of gluconeogenesis to create more glucose</li>
</ul>
<p>Lactic acidosis- Excessive production of lactate can lead to lactic acidosis, a sub-type of metabolic acidosis. This is where the pH of the blood has become more acidic due to rising levels of lactate within the body. There are a number of causes for lactic acidosis but broadly it is caused by the body being unable to respire aerobically. Some causes include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Diabetes mellitus</li>
<li>Enzyme deficiencies – for example pyruvate dehydrogenase deficiency</li>
<li>Drugs – for example, metformin and isoniazid</li>
<li>Haemorrhage</li>
<li>Sepsis</li>
<li>Mitochondrial disorders</li>
</ul>
<p>Symptoms typical of metabolic acidosis include nausea, vomiting, muscle weakness and rapid breathing. Treatment is difficult, as there is little evidence to support the use of sodium bicarbonate solutions (to balance the pH) or direct removal of lactate (via haemofiltration). Treatment is therefore supportive and would depend on the aetiology; if medication is the cause it may need to be withdrawn and certain mitochondrial disorders may require adapted diets. Excessive anaerobic glycolysis produces large quantities of lactic acid. This can exit the cell and enter the bloodstream, and in sufficient amounts can cause lactic acidosis. At this point, serum pH is reduced which can lead to organ dysfunction if severe and untreated.</p>
<p>Cancer and Glycolysis- Tumour cells have a very high rate of glycolysis. This is advantageous to the tumour if it outgrows its blood supply, as it can quickly produce energy from anaerobic glycolysis, allowing fast tumour growth. It is also clinically advantageous in the detection and treatment of cancer. PET scans depict radioactive glycolytic intermediates in cancer cells, allowing visual detection of metastases. Drugs targeted at glycolysis are also used in the treatment of cancer in chemotherapy. An example is imatinib (Gleevec) which reduces the synthesis of hexokinase so that less glucose is trapped in the cell.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Aerobic respiration- takes place in the mitochondria matrix. Pyruvate is transported to the mitochondria matrix from the cytosol and then converted to acetyl coenzyme A by pyruvate dehydrogenase complex. Acetylcoenzyme A (acetylCoA) joins the tricarboxylic acid pathway and through a series of oxidation reactions (loss of electrons and</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>hydrogens) forms reducing equivalents NADH and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">F</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{FADH}_2</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">FADH</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> which donate their electrons to the electron transport chain</p>
<p>Tricarboxylic acid pathway (TCA), Krebs cycle or citric acid pathway - While the primary role of the TCA cycle is production of NADH and <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">F</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{FADH}_2</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">FADH</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>, it also produces molecules that supply various biosynthetic processes. These enter or exit the cycle at various points depending on demand. For example, alpha-ketoglutarate can leave the cycle to be converted into amino acids, and succinate can be converted to haem synthesis. Each cycle produces:</p>
<ul>
<li>Two molecules of carbon dioxide.</li>
<li>Three molecules of NADH.</li>
<li>Three hydrogen ions <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi mathvariant="bold">H</mi><mo>+</mo><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\mathbf{H} +)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord mathbf">H</span><span class="mord">+</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>.</li>
<li>One molecule of <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="bold">F</mi><mi mathvariant="bold">A</mi><mi mathvariant="bold">D</mi><mi mathvariant="bold">H</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathbf{FADH}_2</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8361em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathbf">FADH</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></li>
<li>One molecule of GTP.</li>
</ul>
<p>Each molecule of glucose produces two molecules of pyruvate, which in turn produce two molecules of acetyl-coA. Therefore, each molecule of glucose produces double the net output of each cycle. The TCA Cycle is regulated Metabolites: Products of the cycle provide negative feedback on the enzymes that catalyse it. For example, NADH inhibits the majority of the enzymes found in the TCA cycle. Citrate: Inhibits phosphofructokinase, a key enzyme in glycolysis. This reduces the rate of production of pyruvate and therefore of acetyl-coA. Calcium: Accelerates the TCA cycle by stimulating the link reaction.</p>
<p>Defects of the TCA Cycle - There are in fact no known defects of the TCA cycle that are compatible with life. This highlights the importance of this step in ATP production for sustaining life.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-9d8e389d.jpeg" alt="img-37.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="electron-transport-chain-and-oxidative-phosphorylation">Electron transport chain and oxidative phosphorylation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#electron-transport-chain-and-oxidative-phosphorylation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Electron transport chain consists of four protein complexes I, II, III and IV embedded in the inner mitochondria membrane. Each contains structural features that allow complexes to readily accept and release electrons. Complex I accepts electrons from NADH and complex II from <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">F</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">D</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi></mrow><mn>2</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{FADH}_2</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">FADH</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>. The electrons flow through a series of electron acceptors and donor including cytochromes and the final electron acceptor is oxygen which is converted to water. Oxygen accepts its electrons from complex IV (cytochrome oxidase). Inhibitors like cyanide, azide and carbon monoxide inhibit cytochrome oxidase and are lethal poisons. The electrons, meanwhile, combine with the hydrogen ions and oxygen to form water by Complex IV. However, this process is not perfect. Electrons can leak out of electron transport chain and can reduce oxygen, which can produce free radicals such as superoxide and hydrogen peroxide.</p>
