<p>Oxytocin and antidiuretic hormone (ADH or vasopressin) are the hormones synthesised in the hypothalamus and then released from the axon terminals within the posterior pituitary gland (Fig. 9.3B). These hormones act directly on non-endocrine tissue and their release by exocytosis is stimulated by nerve impulses from the hypothalamus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="oxytocin">Oxytocin<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#oxytocin" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Oxytocin stimulates two target tissues during and after parturition (childbirth): uterine smooth muscle and the muscle cells of the lactating breast. During parturition increasing amounts of oxytocin are released by the posterior pituitary into the bloodstream in response to increasing distension of sensory stretch receptors in the uterine cervix by the baby's head. Sensory impulses are generated and travel to the control centre in the hypothalamus, stimulating the posterior pituitary to release more oxytocin. In turn this stimulates more forceful uterine contractions and greater</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>stretching of the uterine cervix as the baby's head is forced further downwards. This is an example of a positive feedback mechanism which stops soon after the baby is delivered when distension of the uterine cervix is greatly reduced</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-a2cf90d6.jpeg" alt="img-55.jpeg">
Figure 9.5 Regulation of secretion of oxytocin through a positive feedback mechanism.</p>
<p>The process of milk ejection also involves a positive feedback mechanism. Suckling generates sensory impulses that are transmitted from the breast to the hypothalamus. The impulses trigger the release of oxytocin from the posterior pituitary and oxytocin stimulates contraction of the myoepithelial cells around the glandular cells and ducts of the lactating breast to contract, ejecting milk. Suckling also inhibits the release of prolactin inhibiting hormone (PIH), prolonging prolactin secretion and lactation. The role of this hormone in males and non-lactating females remains unclear.</p>
<p>Antidiuretic hormone (ADH) or vasopressin- The main effect of antidiuretic hormone is to reduce urine output (diuresis is the production of a large volume of urine). ADH increases the permeability to water of the distal convoluted and collecting tubules of the nephrons of the kidneys. As a result the reabsorption of water from the glomerular filtrate is increased. The amount of ADH secreted is influenced by the osmotic pressure of the blood circulating to the osmoreceptors in the hypothalamus. As the osmotic pressure rises, the secretion of ADH increases as in, for example, dehydration and following haemorrhage. More water is therefore reabsorbed and the urine output is reduced. This means that the body retains more water and the rise in osmotic pressure is reversed. Conversely, when the osmotic pressure of the blood is low, for example after a large fluid intake, secretion of ADH is reduced, less water is reabsorbed and more urine is produced. At high concentrations, for example after severe blood loss, ADH causes smooth muscle contraction, especially vasoconstriction in the blood vessels of the skin and abdominal organs. This has a pressor effect, raising systemic blood pressure; the alternative name of this hormone, vasopressin, reflects this effect.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="thyroid-gland">Thyroid gland<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#thyroid-gland" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>CALCITONIN- This hormone is secreted by the parafollicular or C-cells in the thyroid gland. It acts on bone and the kidneys to reduce the blood calcium <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(\mathrm{Ca}^{2+})</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.1373em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">Ca</span></span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8873em;"><span style="top: -3.1362em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">2</span><span class="mord mtight">+</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> level when it is raised. It reduces the reabsorption of calcium from bones and inhibits reabsorption of calcium by the renal tubules. Its effect is opposite to that of parathyroid hormone (PTH, parathormone), the hormone secreted by the parathyroid glands. Release of calcitonin is stimulated by an increase in the blood calcium level. This hormone is important during childhood when bones undergo considerable changes in size and shape.</p>
<p>PARATHYROID GLANDS- There are four small parathyroid glands, two embedded in the posterior surface of each lobe of the thyroid gland. They are surrounded by fine connective tissue capsules. Have 2 types of cells Chief cells- The role of this cell type is to secrete parathyroid hormone. They contain prominent Golgi apparatus and endoplasmic reticulum to allow for the synthesis and secretion of parathyroid hormone. The chief cells are the</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>61</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>smaller of the two cell types, however, they are more abundant. <em>Oxyphil cells</em> – These cells are much larger but their purpose is unknown.</p>
<p>Function- The parathyroid glands secrete parathyroid hormone (PTH, parathormone). Secretion is regulated by the blood level of calcium. When this falls, secretion of PTH is increased and vice versa. The main function of PTH is to increase the blood calcium level when it is low. This is achieved by indirectly increasing the amount of calcium absorbed from the small intestine and reabsorbed from the renal tubules. If these sources provide inadequate supplies then PTH stimulates osteoclasts (bone-destroying cells) and resorption of calcium from bones.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-8acaeedf.jpeg" alt="img-56.jpeg"></p>
<p>Parathormone and calcitonin from the thyroid gland act in a complementary manner to maintain blood calcium levels within the normal range. This is needed for:</p>
<ul>
<li>Muscle contraction</li>
<li>blood clotting</li>
<li>Nerve impulse transmission.</li>
</ul>
<p>ADRENAL (SUPRARENAL) GLANDS There are two adrenal glands, one situated on the upper pole of each kidney enclosed within the renal fascia. They are about 4 cm long and 3 cm thick. The arterial blood supply to the glands is by branches from the abdominal aorta and renal arteries.</p>
<p>The venous return is by suprarenal veins. The right gland drains into the inferior vena cava and the left into the left renal vein. The glands are composed of two parts which have different structures and functions. The outer part is the cortex and the inner part the medulla. The adrenal cortex is essential to life but the medulla is not.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="adrenal-cortex">Adrenal cortex<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adrenal-cortex" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The adrenal cortex produces three groups of steroid hormones from cholesterol. They are collectively called adrenocorticocoids (corticosteroids, corticoids). They are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Glucocorticoids</li>
<li>Mineralocorticoids</li>
<li>Sex hormones (androgens).</li>
</ul>
<p>The hormones in each group have different characteristic actions but due to their structural similarity their actions may overlap.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="glucocorticoids">Glucocorticoids<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#glucocorticoids" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cortisol (hydrocortisone), corticosterone and cortisone are the main glucocorticoids. They are essential for life, regulating metabolism and responses to stress. Secretion is stimulated by ACTH from the anterior pituitary and by stress. In non-stressful conditions secretion has marked circadian variations. The highest level of hormones occurs between 4 a.m. and 8 a.m. and the lowest, between midnight and 3 a.m. When the sleeping and waking pattern is changed it takes several days for adjustment of the ACTH/cortisol secretion to take place. Glucocorticoids have widespread effects and these include:</p>
<ul>
<li>gluconeogenesis (formation of new sugar from, for example, protein) and hyperglycaemia (raised blood glucose level)</li>
<li>lipolysis (breakdown of triglycerides into fatty acids and glycerol for energy production)</li>
<li>stimulating breakdown of protein, releasing amino acids, which can be used for synthesis of other proteins, e.g. enzymes, or for energy (ATP) production</li>
<li>promoting absorption of sodium and water from renal tubules (a weak mineralocorticoid effect).</li>
</ul>
<p>In pathological and pharmacological quantities glucocorticoids:</p>
<ul>
<li>have an anti-inflammatory action</li>
<li>suppress the immune response</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>suppress the response of tissues to injury</li>
<li>Delay wound healing</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-38ab98c7.jpeg" alt="img-57.jpeg">
Figure 9.10 Regulation of glucocorticoid secretion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mineralocorticoids-aldosterone">Mineralocorticoids (aldosterone)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mineralocorticoids-aldosterone" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Aldosterone is the main mineralocorticoid. Its functions are associated with the maintenance of water and electrolyte balance in the body. It stimulates the reabsorption of sodium (Na⁺) by the renal tubules and excretion of potassium (K⁺) in the urine. Sodium reabsorption is also accompanied by retention of water and therefore aldosterone is involved in the regulation of blood volume and blood pressure too. The blood potassium level regulates the amount of aldosterone produced by the adrenal cortex. When the blood potassium level rises, more aldosterone is secreted. Low blood potassium has the opposite effect. Angiotensin (see below) also stimulates the release of aldosterone</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-f575d8de.jpeg" alt="img-58.jpeg">
Figure 9.11 Regulation of aldosterone secretion.</p>
<p>Renin-angiotensin-aldosterone system when renal blood flow is reduced or blood sodium levels fall the enzyme renin is secreted by kidney cells. Renin converts the plasma protein angiotensinogen, produced by the liver, to angiotensin 1. Angiotensin converting enzyme (ACE), formed in small quantities in the lungs, proximal kidney tubules and other tissues converts angiotensin 1 to angiotensin 2, which stimulates secretion of aldosterone from adrenal cortex. It also causes vasoconstriction and increases blood pressure.</p>
<p>Sex hormones- Sex hormones secreted by the adrenal cortex are mainly androgens (male sex hormones) and the amounts produced are insignificant compared with those secreted by the testes and ovaries in late puberty and</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>63</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>adulthood. Their role is unclear but it is thought that they contribute to the onset of puberty. An elevated level in females causes masculinisation. Control of secretion is poorly understood.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="adrenal-medulla">Adrenal medulla<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adrenal-medulla" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The medulla is completely surrounded by the cortex. It develops from nervous tissue in the embryo and is part of the sympathetic division of the autonomic nervous system. It is stimulated by its extensive sympathetic nerve supply to produce the hormones adrenaline and nor adrenaline.</p>
<p><strong>Adrenaline and noradrenaline</strong>- Noradrenaline is the postganglionic neurotransmitter of the sympathetic division of the autonomic nervous system. Adrenaline and some noradrenaline are released into the blood from the adrenal medulla during stimulation of the sympathetic nervous system. They are structurally very similar and this explains their similar effects. Together they potentiate the fight or flight response after initial sympathetic stimulation by:</p>
<ul>
<li>increasing heart rate</li>
<li>increasing blood pressure</li>
<li>diverting blood to essential organs including the heart, brain and skeletal muscles by dilating their blood vessels and constricting those of less essential organs, such as the skin</li>
<li>increasing metabolic rate</li>
<li>dilating the pupils.</li>
</ul>
<p>Adrenaline has a greater effect on the heart and metabolic processes whereas noradrenaline has more influence on blood vessels.</p>
<p><strong>Response to stress</strong>- When the body is under stress homeostasis is disturbed. To restore it and, in some cases, to maintain life there are immediate and, if necessary, longer-term responses. Stressors include exercise, fasting, fright, temperature changes, infection, disease and emotional disturbances/ situations. The immediate response is sometimes described as preparing for 'fight or flight'. This is mediated by the sympathetic part of the autonomic nervous system and the principal effects are shown. In the longer term, ACTH from the anterior pituitary stimulates the release of glucocorticoids and mineralocorticoids from the adrenal cortex and a more prolonged response to stress occurs.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-ccb10426.jpeg" alt="img-59.jpeg">
Figure 9.12 Responses to stressors that threaten homeostasis. CRH = corticotrophin releasing hormone. ACTH = adrenocorticotropic hormone.</p>
<p><strong>PANCREATIC ISLETS</strong>- The cells which make up the pancreatic islets (islets of Langerhans) are found in clusters irregularly distributed throughout the substance of the pancreas. Unlike the exocrine pancreas, which produces pancreatic juice, there are no ducts leading from the clusters of islet cells. Pancreatic hormones are secreted directly into the bloodstream and circulate throughout the body. There are three main types of cells in the pancreatic islets:</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>(alpha) cells that secrete glucagon (beta) cells that secrete insulin, (delta) cells that secrete somatostatin (GHRIH). The normal blood glucose level is between 2.5 and 5.3 mmol/litre (45 to 95 mg/100 ml). Blood glucose levels are controlled mainly by the opposing actions of insulin and glucagon:</li>
<li>Glucagon increases blood glucose levels</li>
<li>Insulin reduces blood glucose levels.</li>
</ul>
<p>Insulin- The main function of insulin is to lower blood levels of absorbed nutrients when they rise above normal. When these nutrients, especially glucose, are in excess of immediate needs insulin promotes storage by:</p>
<ul>
<li>Acting on cell membranes and stimulating uptake and use of glucose by muscle and connective tissue cells</li>
<li>increasing conversion of glucose to glycogen (glycogenesis), especially in the liver and skeletal muscles</li>
<li>accelerating uptake of amino acids by cells, and the synthesis of protein</li>
<li>promoting synthesis of fatty acids and storage of fat in adipose tissue (lipogenesis)</li>
<li>decreasing glycogenolysis</li>
<li>preventing the breakdown of protein and fat, and gluconeogenesis (formation of new sugar from, e.g. protein).</li>
</ul>
<p>Secretion of insulin is stimulated by increased blood glucose and amino acid levels, and gastrointestinal hormones, e.g. gastrin, secretin and cholecystokinin. Secretion is decreased by sympathetic stimulation, glucagon, adrenaline, cortisol and somatostatin (GHRIH) secreted by cells of the pancreatic islets. Insulin is a polypeptide consisting of about 50 amino acids. Amounts are expressed in international units (IU).</p>
<p>Glucagon- The effects of glucagon increase blood glucose levels by stimulating, e.g.:</p>
<ul>
<li>Conversion of glycogen to glucose in the liver and skeletal muscles (glycogenolysis)</li>
<li>Gluconeogenesis.</li>
</ul>
<p>Secretion of glucagon is stimulated by a low blood glucose level and exercise and decreased by somatostatin and insulin.</p>
<p>Somatostatin (GHRIH)- The effect of this hormone, also produced by the hypothalamus, is to inhibit the secretion of both insulin and glucagon. It reduces the rate at which food is absorbed from the contents of the intestine. Somatostatin is also secreted by the hypothalamus and by the intestine.</p>
<p>Gamma Cells- The gamma cells of the islets secrete a 36-amino-acid pancreatic polypeptide, which reduces appetite.</p>
<p>Amylin- Amylin is a peptide of 37 amino acids, which is also secreted by the beta cells of the pancreas. Some of its actions include inhibits the secretion of glucagon, slows the emptying of the stomach, sends a satiety signal to the brain. All of its actions tend to supplement those of insulin, reducing the level of glucose in the blood. A synthetic, modified, form of amylin is used in the treatment of type 2 diabetes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="unit-4-biochemistry-of-special-organs-and-functional-tests">UNIT 4 BIOCHEMISTRY OF SPECIAL ORGANS AND FUNCTIONAL TESTS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#unit-4-biochemistry-of-special-organs-and-functional-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="biochemistry-of-the-brain">BIOCHEMISTRY OF THE BRAIN<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#biochemistry-of-the-brain" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>BLOOD BRAIN BARRIER- The choroid plexus is a vascular tissue found in all cerebral ventricles. Instead, the blood—CSF barrier at the choroid plexus is formed by the epithelial cells and the tight junctions that link them. The other part of the blood—CSF barrier is the arachnoid membrane, which envelops the brain. Usually glucose is the only substance metabolized by the brain to supply its energy requirements and most of the energy from oxidative breakdown of glucose transferred to ATP. Brain glycogen stores are negligible. Brain is completely dependent on continuous supply of blood glucose and oxygen. Although adult brain forms only 2% of body weight it receives 15% of total blood supply to support its high oxygen utilization. If oxygen supply is cut off for 4-5 minutes or glucose supply cut off for 10-15 minutes brain damage will occur. Stroke involves stoppage of blood supply to regions of the brain and cells in this region deprived of nutrients cease and function and die. A complex group of blood brain barriers mechanism control kind of substance and rate that they enter the extracellular fluid of brain and minimize ability of many harmful substances from reaching neurons. Unlike the non-restricted diffusion of non-protein substances in other body organs. Endothelial cells that line the brain capillaries, the edge of adjacent endothelial cells are completely sealed together by continuous tight junctions so all substances entering or leaving brain must pass through the two plasma membranes and cytoplasm of endothelial cells rather than between cells capillaries in certain areas of brain lack blood brain barrier but account for less than 1% of brain volume. Barrier has both anatomical structure and physiological transport system therefore its selective. Substances that are lipid soluble enter brain quickly by dissolving in lipid component of plasma membrane e.g. morphine has two hydroxyl groups while heroin has two acetyl groups this makes morphine lipid insoluble and heroin highly lipid soluble. Heroin</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>65</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>crosses blood brain barrier mare readily than morphine. Once in brain heroin enzymatically converted to morphine and trapped in brain. Others with rapid acting effect on CNS are drugs like barbiturates, nicotine, caffeine, and alcohol. Polar nutrients like glucose and amino acids etc enter brain quite rapidly by combining with membrane transport proteins which also transport polar substance out of brain. CNS is perfused by cerebrospinal fluid which is a clear fluid that fills cerebral ventrical and subrachnoid space. Which surrounds brain and spinal chord and protect the soft and delicate tissues of CNS from sudden and jarring movement. Glycerol Constituent of cellular membrane (phospholipid) and is Released following cellular death. Fatty acids poorly cross blood brain barrier except essential fatty acids which have transporters.</p>
<p>BIOCHEMISTRY OF THE PANCREASE- The pancreas has two main functions the endocrine function which produces the hormones and the exocrine function which produces digestive juices full of enzymes. The liver also produces sodium bicarbonate which neutralizes the acidic chime from the stomach as it enters the small intestines.</p>
<p>BIOCHEMISTRY OF ERYTHROCYTES- Erythrocytes are small compared with most other cells and are peculiar because of their biconcave disk shape. They have no nucleus, because it is extruded just before the release of the cell into the blood stream from the bone marrow, where the cells develop. Their cytoplasm has no organelles and is full of the protein hemoglobin that binds O₂ and CO₂. In the cytoplasm are other proteins also, namely,</p>
<p>(1) The submembrane cytoskeleton,
(2) Enzymes of the glycolytic and pentose phosphate pathways, and
(3) A range of other hydrolytic and special-function enzymes that will not be discussed here. In the membrane are specialized proteins associated with
(1) anion transport, and
(2) The bearing of the carbohydrate cell-surface antigens (blood group substances).</p>
<p>Erythrocytes lack mitochondria therefore can only take part in anaerobic respiration producing lactate. Life of erythrocyte is 120 days. Oxygen in red blood cells can interact with iron to form reactive oxygen species which are very reactive and can damage membrane of erythrocytes causing haemolytic anaemia. Glutathione is a strong antioxidant that neutralize free radicals but it requires NADPH produced by the pentose phosphate pathway. Drugs that increase oxidative stress in RBCs like aspirin, some malarial drugs can cause haemolytic anaemia and jaundice in people deficient in glucose-6-phophate dehydrogenase first enzyme of pentose phosphate pathway.</p>
<p>BIOCHEMISTRY OF ADRENAL GLAND- Adrenal glands are two semilunar or pyramidal structures one each on upper pole of both the kidneys also called suprarenal glands. Each gland consists of two developmentally and physiologically separate part called i) adrenal cortex and i) adrenal medulla consisting 10% of the whole gland forms inner part and produces epinephrine and nor epinephrine. Adrenal cortex occupies outer peripheral portion and is histologically differentiated into three layers</p>
<ul>
<li>Outer Zona glomerulosa</li>
<li>Middle Zona Fasciculata</li>
<li>Inner Zona reticularis</li>
</ul>
<p>All the three layers can produce steroid hormones glucocorticoids but mineralocorticoids synthesis is done by Zona glomerulosa only. Adrenal cortex also produces the androgen. The chief glucocorticoid is cortisol. Cortisol secretion has a diurnal rhythm. Glucocorticoids secretion is stimulated by pituitary corticotropin ACTH which in turn is regulated by hypothalamic corticotropin releasing hormone (CRH). The secretion is controlled by negative feedback inhibition by high blood cortisol. Common function tests are</p>
<ol>
<li>Provocative tests</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>ACTH stimulation test Synachen test</li>
<li>Cotrosyn (Tetracosactrin test)</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Test using suppression or inhibition</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Use of cortisone or cortisone like acting steroids</li>
<li>Dexamethasone suppression test</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="biochemistry-of-the-liver">BIOCHEMISTRY OF THE LIVER<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#biochemistry-of-the-liver" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The liver is the largest internal organ, weighing about 1.5 kg (3.3 lb) in the adult. The blood flow to the liver is 1350 ml/min (27% of the cardiac output) on average and comes from two sources:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Hepatic artery</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Hepatic portal vein</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The hepatic artery supplies the liver with 300 ml/min of oxygenated blood from the aorta. The remaining 1050 ml/min of blood flow is delivered by the hepatic portal vein. This blood comes directly from the digestive tract. It is low in oxygen but contains a high concentration of nutrients absorbed from the intestines. The liver performs many important functions, including:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Storage of blood</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Filtration of blood</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Storage of vitamins and iron</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Formation of blood coagulation factors</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Metabolism and excretion of certain drugs, bilirubin, and hormones</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Metabolism of carbohydrates, proteins, and lipids</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Formation of bile</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The liver is a large and distensible organ. As such, large quantities of blood may be stored in its blood vessels providing a blood reservoir function. Under normal physiological conditions, the hepatic veins and hepatic sinuses contain approximately 450 ml of blood, or almost 10% of blood volume. When needed, this blood may be mobilized to increase venous return and cardiac output. Blood flowing from the intestines to the liver through the hepatic portal vein often contains bacteria. Filtration of this blood is a protective function provided by the liver. Large phagocytic macrophages, referred to as Kupffer cells, line the hepatic venous sinuses. As the blood flows through these sinuses, bacteria are rapidly taken up and digested by the Kupffer cells. This system is very efficient and removes more than 99% of the bacteria from the hepatic portal blood.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The liver serves as an important storage site for vitamins and iron. Sufficient quantities of several vitamins may be stored so as to prevent vitamin deficiency for some period of time:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Vitamin A: up to 10 months</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Vitamin D: 3 to 4 months</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Vitamin B12: at least 1 year</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Iron is stored in the liver in the form of ferritin. -Mineral Storage- Iron and copper minerals are stored in the liver. Iron levels within the body need to be tightly regulated. Therefore, excess amounts must be stored in places such as the liver. Most iron within cells is stored in ferritin, a protein produced by the liver. All cell types within the liver can store iron however the majority is stored within hepatocytes. In severe iron overload the ferritin storage becomes saturated and so excess becomes stored within haemosiderin. However haemosiderin is a large, insoluble complex and iron stored within it is difficult to mobilise effectively.</p>
<p>The liver is capable of detoxifying or excreting into the bile many drugs, such as sulfonamides (antibacterial drugs), penicillin, ampicillin, and erythromycin.</p>
<p>-Bilirubin, the major end-product of hemoglobin degradation, is also excreted in the bile. In addition, several hormones are metabolized by the liver, including thyroid hormone and all of the steroid hormones, such as estrogen, cortisol, and aldosterone. In terms of nutrients, the liver is the most important metabolic organ in the body. It receives a large volume of nutrient-rich blood directly from the digestive tract, which provides an abundant amount of substrates for metabolism.</p>
<p>-Metabolic processes involving carbohydrates include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Storage of a significant amount of glycogen- excess glucose can be stored within the liver as glycogen. This is stimulated by insulin release. Around 100g of glycogen is stored in the liver (300g is stored in skeletal muscle).</li>
<li>Conversion of galactose and fructose into glucose</li>
<li>Gluconeogenesis help to control blood sugar (prevent hypoglycemia)</li>
</ul>
<p>-Metabolic processes involving proteins include the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Transamination and Deamination of amino acids</li>
<li>Formation of urea (for removal of ammonia from body fluid)</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>67</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Formation of plasma proteins</li>
<li>Conversion of amino acids into other amino acids and essential compounds</li>
</ul>
<p>Liver proteins- Protein synthesis is stimulated by <strong>insulin</strong> and <strong>growth hormone</strong>. Some of the most important plasma proteins are synthesized in the liver.</p>
<ul>
<li>Albumin</li>
<li>Globulin</li>
<li>Fibrinogen</li>
<li>CRP (an infection marker)-</li>
<li>Clotting factors – Factors II, VII, IX and X are <strong>Vitamin K dependent</strong></li>
<li>Thrombopoietin (TPO) is the principal physiologic regulator of platelet production.</li>
<li>Angiotensinogen</li>
</ul>
<p>Plasma proteins synthesized by the liver play an important role in maintaining the amino acid equilibrium in the blood. In times of tissue amino acid depletion, these proteins can be degraded and released back into the blood as amino acids for tissues to use in protein synthesis. It is also vital to note that the plasma proteins provide oncotic pressure in the blood, meaning they hold water in the plasma.</p>
<p>-Most cells in the body metabolize lipids; however, some processes of fat metabolism occur mainly in the liver. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Oxidation of fatty acids to supply energy for other body functions</li>
<li>Synthesis of cholesterol, phospholipids, fatty acids, triglycerides and lipoproteins</li>
<li>Synthesis of fat from proteins and carbohydrates</li>
</ul>
<p>Another important product of liver metabolism is the bile necessary for digestion and absorption of dietary lipids. Bile is an aqueous, alkaline fluid consisting of a complex mixture of organic and inorganic components. The major organic constituents of bile are the bile salts, which account for approximately 50% of the solid components. Derived from cholesterol, bile salts are amphipathic molecules; in other words, these molecules have a hydrophilic region and a hydrophobic region. Inorganic ions are also present in the bile and include Na⁺, K⁺, Ca²⁺, Cl⁻, and HCO₃⁻ ions. The total number of cations exceeds the total number of anions.</p>
<p>-Bile is produced continuously by the liver; bile salts are secreted by the hepatocytes; and the water, sodium bicarbonate, and other inorganic salts are added by the cells of the bile ducts within the liver. The bile is then transported by way of the common bile duct to the duodenum. Bile facilitates fat digestion and absorption throughout the length of the small intestine. In the terminal region of the ileum, the final segment of the small intestine, the bile salts are actively reabsorbed into the blood, returned to the liver by way of the hepatic portal system, and resecreted into the bile. This recycling of the bile salts from the small intestine back to the liver is referred to as enterohepatic circulation.</p>
<p>Bile secretion by the liver is stimulated by:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Bile salts</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Secretin</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Parasympathetic stimulation</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The return of the bile salts to the liver from the small intestine is the most potent stimulus of bile secretion. In fact, these bile salts may cycle two to five times during each meal. The intestinal hormone secretin, which is released in response to acid in the duodenum, enhances aqueous alkaline secretion by the liver. Secretin has no effect on the secretion of bile salts. During the cephalic phase of digestion, before food even reaches the stomach or intestine, parasympathetic stimulation, by way of the vagus nerve, promotes bile secretion from the liver.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>FUNCTIONS OF THE LIVER -The liver is involved in metabolism and independently involved in many other biochemical functions. The regenerating power of liver cells is tremendous.</p>
<ol>
<li>Metabolic function- liver is the key organ and principle site where the metabolism of carbohydrates, lipids, proteins, takes place.</li>
<li>Liver is the organ where ammonia is converted to urea</li>
<li>It is the principle organ where cholesterol is synthesized and catabolized to form bile and bile salts</li>
<li>Esterification of cholesterol takes place solely in the liver</li>
<li>In this organ absorbed monosaccharides other than glucose are converted to glucose i.e. galactose is converted to glucose, fructose is concerted to glucose.</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Liver besides other organs can bring about catabolism and anabolism of nucleic acids</li>
<li>Liver is also involved in metabolism of vitamins and minerals to certain extent.</li>
<li>Secretory function- liver is responsible for the formation and secretion of bile in the intestines. Bile pigments bilirubin formed from heme catabolism is conjugated in liver cells and secreted in bile</li>
<li>Excretory function- certain exogenous dyes like BPS (bromsulphthalein) and rose Bengal dye are exclusively excreted through liver cells</li>
<li>Synthesis of certain blood coagulation factors- liver cells are responsible for conversion of preprothrombin (inactive) to active prothrombin in the presence of vitamin K. it also produces other clotting factors like factor V, VII, and X. Fibrinogen involved in blood coagulation is also synthesized in liver</li>
<li>Synthesis of other proteins - Albumin is solely synthesized in the liver and also to some extent  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span>  and  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>β</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\beta</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05278em;">β</span></span></span></span></span>  globulins.</li>
<li>Detoxification function and protective function- Kupffer cells of liver remove foreign bodies from blood by phagocytosis. Liver cells can detoxificate drugs, hormones, and convert them into less toxic substances for excretion.</li>
<li>Storage function - liver stores glucose in the form of glycogen. It also stores vitamin  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi><mn>12</mn></msub></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{B}_{12}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8333em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">B</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">12</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.15em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> , A etc.</li>
<li>Miscellaneous function- liver is involved in blood formation in embryo and in some abnormal states, it also forms blood in adults. vitamin-D3 is converted into 25-hydroxyvitamin-D by 25-hydroxylase.</li>
</ol>
<p>Types of liver cells- There are 4 basic cell types that reside in the liver:</p>
<ol>
<li>The hepatocyte- parenchymal cells and form  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>80</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">80\%</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8056em; vertical-align: -0.0556em;"></span><span class="mord">80%</span></span></span></span></span>  of liver cells, store glucose in the form of glycogen, also vitamin B12, folic acid and iron, participate in the turnover and transport of lipids, synthesize some of the plasma proteins (albumin,  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span>  and  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>β</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\beta</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; vertical-align: -0.1944em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.05278em;">β</span></span></span></span></span>  globulins, prothrombin, fibrinogen, metabolize/detoxify fat, participate in the turnover of steroid hormones, regulates cholesterol level, secrete bile.</li>
<li>The stellate- fat storing cells. store approximately  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>80</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">%</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">80\%</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8056em; vertical-align: -0.0556em;"></span><span class="mord">80%</span></span></span></span></span>  of the body's supply of vitamin A and a variety of other lipids</li>
<li>The Kupffer cell- macrophages clear the blood of ingested bacterial pathogens that may enter portal blood from the gut.</li>
<li>Pit cells - are liver-specific natural killer (NK) cells and belong to the group of sinusoidal cells</li>
<li>The sinusoidal endothelial cell-form the wall of the blood vessels (sinusoids) that carry blood throughout the liver</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="bilirubin-metabolism">Bilirubin Metabolism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bilirubin-metabolism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Creation of Bilirubin- The life of hemoglobin is about same as RbC which is 120 days. Reticuloendothelial cells are macrophages which are responsible for the maintenance of the blood, through the destruction of old or abnormal cells in the liver and spleen where they are trapped. They take up red blood cells and metabolise the haemoglobin present into its individual components; haem and globin. Globin is further broken down into amino acids which are subsequently recycled. Meanwhile, haem is broken down into iron and biliverdin, a process which is catalysed by haem oxygenase. The iron gets recycled, while biliverdin is reduced to create unconjugated bilirubin. Biliburin and Biliverdin are also known as unconjugated bilirubin while Bilirubin mono and di- glucoronide are known as conjugated bilirubin</li>
<li>Bilirubin Conjugation- In the bloodstream, unconjugated bilirubin binds to albumin to facilitate its transport to the liver. Hepatocytes trap bilirubin by means of specific binding protein called ligadin. Once in the liver, glucuronic acid is added to unconjugated bilirubin by the enzyme glucuronyl transferase. This forms conjugated bilirubin which include Bilirubin mono- and di-glucoronide, which is soluble and polar. This allows conjugated bilirubin to be excreted into the duodenum in bile.</li>
</ol>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251216-6452c511.jpeg" alt="img-60.jpeg"></p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Bilirubin Excretion- Once in the colon, colonic bacteria deconjugate bilirubin and convert it into urobilinogen. Around 80% of this urobilinogen is further oxidised by intestinal bacteria and converted to stercobilin and then excreted through faeces. It is stercobilin which gives faeces their colour. Around 20% of the urobilinogen is reabsorbed into the bloodstream as part of the enterohepatic circulation. It is carried to the liver where some is recycled for bile production, while a small percentage reaches the kidneys. Here, it is oxidised further into urobilin and then excreted into the urine. Bilirubin diglucoronide is poorly absorbed by intestines mucosa glucuronide residues are also released in terminal ileum and large intestines by intestinal beta-glucoronase and anaerobic bacteria to colourless linear tetrapyrole stercobilinogen, mesobilinogen, urobilinogen. This compounds collectively are called urobilinogen. and excreted in faeces. Some of the urobilinogen reabsorbed passively from colon (2%) and return to liver via portal vein where it is re excreted in bile. A small portion of reabsorbed urobilinogen (2-5%) excreted in urine as urobilin.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="liver-function-tests">LIVER FUNCTION TESTS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#liver-function-tests" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Liver function tests are a group of tests done to assess the functional capacity of the liver as well as any cellular damage to the liver cells. To assess all functional capabilities of the liver such as:</p>
<p>(a) Its Synthetic ability: By measuring the various plasma proteins such as albumin and prothrombin that are synthesized by the liver. Also lipids which are also synthesized in the liver.
(b) Its secretory/excretory abilities: By measuring the serum bilirubin level</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="classification">CLASSIFICATION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Tests used in the study of patients with liver and biliary tract diseases can be classified according to the specific functions of the liver involved</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>I. Tests based on abnormalities of pigment metabolism: a. Serum bilirubin and VD Bergh reaction b. Icteric index c. Urine bilirubin d. Urine and faecal urobilinogen e. Serum and urinary bile acids. II. Tests based on liver's part in carbohydrate metabolism: a. Galactose tolerance test b. Fructose tolerance test. III. Tests based on changes in plasma proteins: a. Estimation of total plasma proteins, albumin and globulin and determination of A:G ratio b. Determination of plasma fibrinogen c. Various flocculation tests. d. Amino acids in urine.</th><th>IV. Tests based on abnormalities of lipids: a. Determination of serum cholesterol and ester cholesterol and their ratio b. Determination of faecal fats. V. Tests based on detoxicating function of liver: a. Hippuric acid synthesis test b. The amino anti-pyrimeme breath test. VI. Excretion of injected substances by the liver (excretory function): a. Bromsulphalein test (BSP-retention test) b. 131I Rose Bengal test. VII. Formation of prothrombin by liver: a. Determination of prothrombin time. VIII. Tests based on amino acid catabolism: a. Determination of blood NH3 b. Determination of glutamine in CS fluid (Indirect Liver Function Test). IX. Determination of serum enzyme activities.</th></tr></thead></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-common-tests-that-form-part-of-the-liver-function-test-profile">THE COMMON TESTS THAT FORM PART OF THE LIVER FUNCTION TEST PROFILE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-common-tests-that-form-part-of-the-liver-function-test-profile" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>(a) Serum Bilirubin: both conjugated and unconjugated.
(b) Total serum proteins and albumin globulin ratio.
(c) Liver enzymes: Transaminases: AST (SGOT), ALT (SGPT). Others: ALP, GGT, LDH.
(d) Prothrombin time</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="serum-bilirubin">SERUM BILIRUBIN<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#serum-bilirubin" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Bilirubin is one of the end products of haem metabolism and is derived from the haem part of the hemoglobin molecule. It is a yellow coloured pigment. Liver plays an important role in the metabolism of bilirubin. After the breakdown of haem portion of the hemoglobin molecule 'unconjugated bilirubin' is insoluble in water. It is transferred from the site of RBC and haem breakdown such as the spleen to the liver for 'conjugation' bound to albumin. At the liver it is conjugated with 2-glucoronic acid with the help of enzyme glucuronyl transferase. This conjugation makes bilirubin water soluble and this conjugated bilirubin is excreted into the bile. While measuring bilirubin we measure total and conjugated bilirubin (Direct bilirubin) and calculate the indirect bilirubin by subtracting the direct from the total.</p>
<p>The normal range of bilirubin is:</p>
<ul>
<li>Total Bilirubin: 0.2 to 1 mg/dl</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Unconjugated Bilirubin: 0.1 to 0.6 mg/dl</li>
<li>Conjugated bilirubin: 0.1 to 0.4 mg/dl</li>
</ul>
<p>A rise of bilirubin level to that of 2 mg/dl results in the symptoms of jaundice which is marked by deposition of bilirubin in the various mucous membranes. Jaundice- Jaundice describes a yellow discolouration of the sclera and/or skin in response to elevated bilirubin levels. Jaundice is divided into three types depending on its etiology:</p>
<p>(a) Pre- hepatic jaundice: cause is the level of bilirubin processing before it reaches the liver. Most common cause is over production of bilirubin due to hemolytic disorders. In this case the rise in the level of unconjugated bilirubin is more than conjugated bilirubin hence there is a rise in total and indirect bilirubin. This is caused by</p>
<ul>
<li>Tropical disease, e.g. malaria, yellow fever</li>
<li>Genetic disorders, e.g. sickle-cell anaemia, Gilbert’s syndrome</li>
<li>Haemolytic anaemias</li>
</ul>
<p>(b) Hepatic jaundice: This is caused by cellular dysfunction of the liver hence is also called hepatocellular jaundice. It is caused by the inability of the liver cells to process and excrete the bilirubin in the system. It is seen in hepatitis, cirrhosis of liver etc. In this jaundice there is rise in total, direct as well as indirect bilirubin levels. Liver damage can result from:</p>
<ul>
<li>Viral hepatitis</li>
<li>Hepatotoxic drugs, e.g. paracetamol overdose, alcohol abuse</li>
</ul>
<p>(c) Post-hepatic jaundice: This is also known as obstructive jaundice as it is caused by obstruction to the outflow of bile resulting in reabsorption of conjugated bilirubin and it making an appearance in the serum. It causes by carcinoma of the mouth of gall bladder, stone in the bile duct etc. In this type of jaundice we see a rise in total as well as direct (conjugated) bilirubin. Bile duct obstruction can be caused by:</p>
<ul>
<li>Gallstones</li>
<li>Hepatic tumours</li>
<li>Pancreatic tumours</li>
</ul>
<p>The common method of measuring serum bilirubin level is the Diazo method using Diazotized sufanilic acid to convert bilirubin into a azobilirubin the color intensity of which is measured colorimetrically at a wavelength between 555 nm (550 to 580 nm).</p>
<p>The conjugated bilirubin reacts directly with the Diazosulfanilic acid in an aqueous medium and hence also called direct bilirubin. For unconjugated or free bilirubin which is not water soluble we need to dissolve it into DMSO (Dimethyl sulfoxide) for the reaction to occur. This method is the ‘indirect method’ for measuring bilirubin levels and it measures both conjugated and unconjugated bilirubin i.e. total serum bilirubin.</p>
<p>Gilbert’s Syndrome- Gilbert’s syndrome is an inherited disorder where there is hyperbilirubinaemia (excess bilirubin in the bloodstream) due to a fault in the UGT₁A₁ gene leading to a deficiency in UDP-gluconoryltransferase. This faulty gene results in slower conjugation of bilirubin in the liver and so it builds up in the bloodstream instead of being excreted through the biliary ducts. When well, patients are usually asymptomatic and have normal bilirubin levels. However, under physiological stressors such as illness, alcohol abuse and extreme exercise, patients can become markedly jaundiced.</p>
<p>Bile salts- The main route for excretion of cholesterol from the body is through the liver where some is converted to bile. The conversion of cholesterol to bile salts begins when hydroxyl groups are introduced into the phenanthrene ring of cholesterol by the action of cholesterol 7-a-hydroxylase, followed by modification of the side chain. Cholic acid and chenodeoxycholic acid are produced.</p>
<p>Cholelithiasis: Bile salts and phospholipids are responsible for keeping the cholesterol in bile in a soluble state. Due to their deficiency (particularly bile salts), cholesterol crystals precipitate in the gall bladder often resulting in cholelithiasis-cholesterol gall stone disease Cholelithiasis may be due to defective absorption of bile salts from the intestine, impairment in liver function, obstruction of biliary tract etc. The patients of cholelithiasis respond to the administration of bile acid chenodeoxy cholic acid, commonly known as chenodiol. It is believed that a slow but gradual dissolution of gall stones occurs due to chenodiol. For severe cases of cholelithiasis, surgical removal of gall bladder is the only remedy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="serum-albumin-and-albumin-globulin-ratio">SERUM ALBUMIN AND ALBUMIN GLOBULIN RATIO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#serum-albumin-and-albumin-globulin-ratio" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Serum albumin is an important serum protein vital for maintaining the plasma oncotic pressure as well as acts as a carrier for various biological substances and drugs. Serum albumin is exclusively synthesized by liver and hence the</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>71</p>
<p>Studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>level of serum albumin gives us a stock of the synthetic ability of the liver. Another cause of fall of serum albumin may be protein malnutrition but in that case the fall of all serum proteins including globulins will be seen.</p>
<p>The normal total protein level is 5 to 8.5 gm/dl. The total serum albumin level is 3.5 to 5 gm/dl. The total plasma globulin level is calculated by subtracting the plasma albumin from the total protein level and is normally in the range of 2 to 2.5 gm/dl. The normal range for albumin:globulin ratio is 1.2 to 1.5. But with hepatic dysfunction this ratio recedes towards 1 as the synthetic function of liver is compromised. The reversal of the ratio i.e if the value recedes below 1, it is an ominous sign and may mark an infective/inflammatory pathology marked by rise in serum globulin level and fall in serum albumin levels.</p>
<p>To measure serum albumin the Bromocresol green method is used. Albumin in the presence of bromocresol green at a slightly acidic pH gives a yellow green to blue green colour. The intensity of this colour is dependent on the concentration of albumin in the sample. This intensity is read at a wavelength of 630 nm.</p>
<p>To measure the total protein content of the sample the biuret method it used. In this method the cupric ions of copper (II) sulphate, present in the biuret reagent, form a violet coloured complex with the proteins in a slightly alkaline medium. The intensity of the colour formed is measured at a wavelength of 540 nm (530 to 550 nm).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prothrombin-time">PROTHROMBIN TIME<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prothrombin-time" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prothrombin is a clotting factor (clotting factor II) and it forms an important part of both the intrinsic and extrinsic pathway. Its active form is Thrombin (also clotting factor IIa). It is a serine peptidase which converts fibrinogen to fibrin. Prothrombin is synthesized in the liver. And hence prothrombin activity in plasma is used to measure the synthetic function of liver.</p>
<p>Prothrombin time is measured by taking human plasma from blood that has been collected in tube containing citrate as an anticoagulant. The plasma in put in an automated machine which adds an excess of calcium to reverse the anticoagulant effects of citrates and measures the time taken for fibrinogen to be converted to fibrin hence measures the activity of thrombin in the plasma. The prothrombin time differs in accordance to the analytical method used. Hence to compensate for this International normalized ratio (INR) is used. In this the manufacturers of the kit assign an International sensitivity index (ISI) value. This shows the amount of tissue factor present in the kit as against an internationally accepted standard. The ISI value is generally 1 to 2. The following is the method to calculate INR:</p>
<div class="math math-display"><span class="katex-display"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" display="block"><semantics><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">R</mi></mrow><mo>=</mo><mrow><mo fence="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">T</mi></mrow><mtext>&nbsp;test&nbsp;sample</mtext></mrow><mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">T</mi></mrow><mtext>&nbsp;control</mtext></mrow></mfrac><mo fence="true">)</mo></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">I</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{INR} = \left(\frac{\mathrm{PT} \text{ test sample}}{\mathrm{PT} \text{ control}}\right) \mathrm{ISI}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6833em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">INR</span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span><span class="mrel">=</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2778em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 2.4em; vertical-align: -0.95em;"></span><span class="minner"><span class="mopen delimcenter" style="top: 0em;"><span class="delimsizing size3">(</span></span><span class="mord"><span class="mopen nulldelimiter"></span><span class="mfrac"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 1.3714em;"><span style="top: -2.314em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">PT</span></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">&nbsp;control</span></span></span></span><span style="top: -3.23em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="frac-line" style="border-bottom-width: 0.04em;"></span></span><span style="top: -3.677em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 3em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">PT</span></span><span class="mord text"><span class="mord">&nbsp;test&nbsp;sample</span></span></span></span></span><span class="vlist-s">​</span></span><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.686em;"><span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mclose nulldelimiter"></span></span><span class="mclose delimcenter" style="top: 0em;"><span class="delimsizing size3">)</span></span></span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.1667em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">ISI</span></span></span></span></span></span></div>
<p>A ratio of 0.8 to 1.2 is considered normal for patients not on warfarin. For individuals on warfarin for any disorder an INR of 2.0 to 3.0 is the target.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tests-based-on-excretory-function-of-liver">TESTS BASED ON EXCRETORY FUNCTION OF LIVER<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tests-based-on-excretory-function-of-liver" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-bsp-retention-test-bromsulphalein-test">1. BSP-Retention Test (Bromsulphalein Test)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-bsp-retention-test-bromsulphalein-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="principle">Principle:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#principle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>The ability of the liver to excrete certain dyes, e.g., BSP is utilized in this test.</li>
<li>In normal healthy individual, a constant proportion (10–15% of the dye) is removed per minute. In hepatic damage and insufficiency, BSP removal is impaired by cellular failure, as damaged liver cells fail to conjugate the dye or due to decrease blood flow.</li>
<li>Removal of BSP by the liver involves conjugation of the dye as a mercaptide with the cysteine component of glutathione. The reaction of conjugation of BSP with glutathione is rate-limiting, and thus it exerts a controlling influence on the rate of removal of the dye.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="determination-of-glutamine-in-cerebrospinal-fluid-an-indirect-liver-function-test">Determination of Glutamine in Cerebrospinal Fluid (An Indirect Liver Function Test)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#determination-of-glutamine-in-cerebrospinal-fluid-an-indirect-liver-function-test" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h5 level="5" id="interpretations">Interpretations<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#interpretations" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h5></div>
<ul>
<li>The normal range: found to be 6.0 to 14.0 mg%.</li>
<li>In infectious hepatitis: glutamine was found to range from 16 to 28 mg%, but usually less than 30 mg%.</li>
<li>In cirrhosis liver: the increase is more; depending on the severity. It varied from 22 to 36 mg% or more.</li>
<li>In hepatic coma: increase is very high, ranging from 30 to 60 mg% or more.</li>
<li>In other types of coma: normal values are obtained.</li>
</ul>
<p>Some authorities put 40 mg% as a critical level. Prognosis of the case is fatal if CSF glutamine level is more than 40 mg%, in case of cirrhosis liver and hepatic coma.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="liver-enzymes">LIVER ENZYMES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#liver-enzymes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Liver enzymes along with bilirubin are the most commonly measured parameter measured in the liver function test. These enzymes are hepatic in origin and they are leaked into the serum with the destruction of hepatic cells. Liver</p>
<p>Downloaded by PATRICK NGOBIRO (<a href="mailto:patrickngobiro@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">patrickngobiro@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>enzymes are measured to get an idea of the cellular insult on the liver and are increased in a wide variety of conditions such as viral hepatitis, toxic hepatitis, cirrhosis of liver etc.</p>
<p>The commonly measured enzymes are:</p>
<p>(a) Transaminases: AST (SGOT), ALT (SGPT)
(b) Transpeptidases: GGT
(c) Phosphatase: ALP.</p>
<p>(a) Transaminases: They are a group of enzymes that transfer the amino group from an amino acid to <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span> keto acid converting the <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span> keto acid into an amino acid while converting the amino acid into a keto acid. The transaminases that are measured in the liver function test are ALT and AST.</p>
<p>Alanine transaminase (ALT) catalyses the following reaction:</p>
<p>Alanine + <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mi>α</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\alpha</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.4306em;"></span><span class="mord mathnormal" style="margin-right: 0.0037em;">α</span></span></span></span></span> keto Glutarate <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mover><mo stretchy="true" minsize="3.0em">→</mo><mpadded width="+0.6em" lspace="0.3em"><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">L</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">T</mi></mrow></mpadded></mover></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\xrightarrow{\mathrm{ALT}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.1113em; vertical-align: -0.011em;"></span><span class="mrel x-arrow"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 1.1003em;"><span style="top: -3.322em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight x-arrow-pad"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mathrm mtight">ALT</span></span></span></span></span><span class="svg-align" style="top: -2.689em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="hide-tail" style="height: 0.522em; min-width: 1.469em;"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="400em" height="0.522em" viewBox="0 0 400000 522" preserveAspectRatio="xMaxYMin slice"><path d="M0 241v40h399891c-47.3 35.3-84 78-110 128

<p>☐ The normal level of ALT in serum is 7 to 40 IU/L.
☐ The normal level of AST in serum is 8 to 40 IU/L.</p>
<p>An increase in AST or ALT levels hints at an insult to the liver parenchyma tissue. ALT is a more specific marker of hepatic injury than AST as AST elevation is also seen in cardiac tissue injury, haemolysis and muscle tissue. To measure the level of transaminases the reaction catalysed by them is coupled to a reaction in which NADH is used up resulting in change in the photometric intensity when read in the UV range at <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>340</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">340\mathrm{nm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">340</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">nm</span></span></span></span></span></span>. It is a UV kinetic method.</p>

<p>(b) Alkaline Phosphatase: It is a hydrolase that removes phosphates from all kinds of molecules such as proteins, nucleotides etc.</p>
<p>It is found in cells lining the biliary system hence a rise in its level is indicative of damage to the biliary tree due to cholestasis. It may be due to stone blocking the large ducts or intrahepatic obstruction, inflammation of the biliary channels. Alkaline phosphatase is also found in placenta and bones. Hence the level is also increased in growing children in whom bones undergo remodeling and in Paget's disease in adults. Normal level of alkaline phosphatase is between 45 to 115 IU/L.</p>
<p>The method for measuring the level of alkaline phosphatase is a kinetic method using p-nitrophenylphosphate as substrate for the enzyme and measuring rate of formation of the colored substrate (p-nitrophenol) formed from the reaction. This measurement of the color intensity is done colorimetrically at a wavelength of <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>405</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">405\mathrm{nm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">405</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">nm</span></span></span></span></span></span>.</p>
<p>p-Nitrophenylphosphate + <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msub><mi mathvariant="normal">H</mi><mn>2</mn></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi><mover><mo stretchy="true" minsize="3.0em">→</mo><mpadded width="+0.6em" lspace="0.3em"><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">L</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">P</mi></mrow></mpadded></mover></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\mathrm{H}_2\mathrm{O} \xrightarrow{\mathrm{ALP}}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1.2503em; vertical-align: -0.15em;"></span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">H</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t vlist-t2"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.3011em;"><span style="top: -2.55em; <li>Normal range is 10 to 47 IU/L</li>
<li>Imponent in alcohol abuse. The activity of this microsomal enzyme has been disruption but may be due to enzyme induction by drugs such as phenobarbiton, phenytoin, warfarin, and alcohol. These severe limitations have meant that this test has now only two practical uses. i) An elevated level implies that an elevated ALP is of hepatic origin and (ii) may be useful in screening for alcohol abuse. Sudden increase in levels in chronic alcoholics suggest recent bout of drinking alcohols.</li>
</ul>
